Three Types of Tafawut in Mushakkik Terms
Enumerates and discusses the three types of difference in mushakkik terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between awwaliyya, awlawiyya, and ashaddiyya as types of tafawut in mushakkik and identify which type a given example belongs to.

Core Idea
Tafawut in mushakkik: essential priority, intellectual preference, or intensity.

In the study of mushakkik (graded) terms, logicians have identified three main ways in which the meaning can apply to individuals with difference in degree. This passage from al-Dasuqi's commentary enumerates them: awwaliyya (essential priority), awlawiyya (intellectual preference), and ashaddiyya (intensity). Understanding these distinctions is crucial for precise definition in kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
Locate the three faces of tafawut explicitly mentioned: awwaliyya, awlawiyya, and ashaddiyya. Note that the author initially mentions only two, but the commentary adds a third.
awwaliyya is essential precedence, awlawiyya is intellectual preference, ashaddiyya is intensity.
2
Distinguish awwaliyya from awlawiyya
The passage stresses that awwaliyya is essential precedence (causality), while awlawiyya is appropriateness in the intellect. They are different considerations, even if the prior is often better.
Al-Jalal emphasizes this distinction.
3
Understand ashaddiyya
Ashaddiyya refers to intensity, like whiteness being stronger in snow than in ivory. Mir Zahid explains it describes the individual only, not the universal.
This is separate from awwaliyya and awlawiyya.
4
Apply the distinction
Given a graded term like existence (wujud) or light (nur), classify which type of tafawut applies. For existence, the cause has awwaliyya over the effect; for light, the sun has ashaddiyya over the moon.
Think of examples from kalam.

بأَوَّلِيَّةٍ) الباء سببيَّة . العطار (قَوْلُهُ : إِمَّا بِأَوَّلِيَّةٍ أَوْ أَوْلَوِيَّةٍ قالَ الجَلالُ : لا يُقَالُ الثَّانية تشتمل على الأُوْلَى أيضاً ؛ فإنَّ اتّصافَ العِلَّةِ بِالوجودِ أَوْلَى والأشدّيَّةُ بمعنى أكثرِيَّةِ الآثارِ كَمَا في الأبيض بالنسبة إلى الثَّلْجِ والعَاجِ ، وبَقِيَ قِسم رابع ذكرَهُ الجَلالُ في حاشية التجريدِ وهو الزيادة والنقصان، لكنَّه غيرُ شَهير، والمصنِّفُ اكتفى بالأَوَّلَيْنِ؛ لأنَّ الثَّالِثَ يَستلزمُ الثَّاني؛ فَاتَّجَهَ عليهِ أَنَّهُ لَمَّا جعلَ الأَوَّليَّةَ قِسْماً بِرَأْسِهِ مُقَابِلا لِلأولويَّةِ مَعَ كَوْنِ الأوليةِ مُسْتَمِلَةٌ عليها لأجل أنَّ ؛ الأَوَّل : مَا يَتَصفُ الفردُ فقط وَلَا يَتَّصِفُ بِهِ صِدْقُ الكُلّيّ عليه كالأشدّيَّةِ والثَّاني : مَا يَتَصفُ بِهِ الصِّدقُ فقط وَهُوَ الأَوَّليَّةُ ، والثَّالث : مَا يَتَصفُ بِهِ الفردُ نَبَّهُوا عَليهِ؛ لَا الزَّمانيُّ ؛ تدبَّر، وبقي هَهُنا بحث وهو
Chapter: On discussions of words. And that difference is either by priority (awwaliyya) like existence. Al-Dasuqi: (His saying: And that) meaning: the difference illustrated by the occurrence of the meaning in some individuals being more prior (awla) than its occurrence in others. (His saying: by priority) the ba is causal. Al-Attar: (His saying: either by priority or preference) al-Jalal said: It cannot be said that the second includes the first also; for the attribution of existence to the cause is more appropriate (awla) than its attribution to the effect; since it is not hidden that the consideration of priority is different from the consideration of preference, even if the prior is better, but it appears from that that intensity (ashaddiyya) is also like that, so let it be made another division. End quote. Meaning: that the well-known in mushakkik is the consideration of difference by one of three ways: awwaliyya meaning precedence in essence, i.e., causality; awlawiyya meaning appropriateness in the view of the intellect; and ashaddiyya meaning more abundant effects, as in white with respect to snow and ivory. There remains a fourth division mentioned by al-Jalal in his gloss on al-Tajrid, which is increase and decrease, but it is not well-known. The author confined himself to the first two because the third entails the second. So it was objected to him that when he made awwaliyya a separate division opposite to awlawiyya, despite awwaliyya including it, because the consideration of awwaliyya is different from the consideration of awlawiyya, then the consideration of ashaddiyya is different from the consideration of awlawiyya, so let it be made another division opposite to both for this consideration. And Mir Zahid answered that mushakkik has three faces: first, what the individual describes only and the truth of the universal does not describe it, like ashaddiyya; second, what the truth describes only, and that is awwaliyya; third, what both the individual and the truth describe together, and awlawiyya is of this kind. The author did not make the first a face of mushakkik because what the individual describes and the truth does not describe is not in reality from the faces of mushakkik, but rather from the necessitators of awlawiyya which is from its faces. End quote. And what is in the marginal gloss of lack of clarity of returning awwaliyya to awlawiyya and that the matter is the opposite is a mistake; for where existence in the Necessary is more perfect and more appropriate (awla) than in the possible, because it is necessary, i.e., no non-being precedes or follows it, it is prior in the view of the intellect and also prior by essential precedence in the external over the existence of the possible. This is the meaning of awwaliyya, for what is meant by it is essential precedence, as they alerted, not temporal; reflect. And there remains here a discussion.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage, part of a chapter on discussions of words, classifies the types of tafawut (difference in degree) in mushakkik terms. The matn states that the difference is either by priority (awwaliyya), like existence. The commentary explains that 'awwaliyya' means essential precedence, as in the cause having existence prior to the effect. Al-Jalal objects that the second type (awlawiyya, preference) might include the first, but they are distinct. He adds a third type: intensity (ashaddiyya), and a fourth (increase/decrease) but it is obscure. The author (of the matn, likely al-Akhdari or similar) only mentions the first two because the third entails the second. A counter-objection is raised: if awwaliyya is separate from awlawiyya because consideration differs, then ashaddiyya should also be separate. Mir Zahid responds that the three types are distinguished by what they describe: ashaddiyya describes the individual only (e.g., whiteness in snow vs. ivory is more intense), awwaliyya describes the truth (universal) only (priority of existence in the cause), and awlawiyya describes both (the individual and the universal). The author's omission of ashaddiyya is because when the truth does not describe the universal, it is not a face of mushakkik but a cause of awlawiyya. The commentary clarifies that awwaliyya is essential, not temporal, priority. An Islamic example: existence (wujud) is awwaliyya in God (Necessary Being) compared to creatures (possible beings) because His existence is self-necessary, not preceded by non-being.

أَوْلَوِيَّة
أَشَدِّيَّة
تَشْكِيك

Key Takeaway
Tafawut in mushakkik terms occurs through awwaliyya (essential priority), awlawiyya (intellectual preference), or ashaddiyya (intensity), distinguished by whether the property describes the individual, the universal, or both.

Definition of Mushtarak and the Condition of Individual Placement
Author defines mushtarak and discusses the condition of multiple placements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a word qualifies as mushtarak by checking if each meaning has a separate individual placement.

Core Idea
Mushtarak requires a separate linguistic stipulation for each meaning, not metaphorical extension.

The author defines mushtarak and emphasizes a key condition: the word must be placed by a distinct act of wad' for each meaning. This precision distinguishes true homonymy from metaphorical usage. In this lesson, we will unpack the definition and the condition that makes it rigorous, using commentary from the sharh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and definiens
Find the term being defined (mushtarak) and the statement that defines it. The definition is: 'a single word is placed for each of its numerous meanings'.
The definiendum is 'mushtarak', the definiens includes the condition of wad' for each meaning.
2
Analyze the condition of wad' shakhsi
The commentator clarifies that the placement must be wad' shakhsi (individual) for each meaning, not the more general wad' naw'i (generic). Understand why this is crucial: without it, metaphorical extensions would be misclassified as mushtarak.
This condition is the core of the definition.
3
Apply the definition to examples
Consider the example 'ayn (eye/spring/spy). Each meaning has a separate original placement, so it is mushtarak. Contrast with 'salat' (du'a vs ritual prayer), where the legal meaning derives metaphorically, lacking a separate placement.
The 'salat' example comes from the quiz; it illustrates the exclusion of metaphorical usage.
4
Recognize the linguistic form of 'mushtarak'
The commentator notes that 'mushtarak' originates from 'mushtarak fihi' with deletion of the preposition. This is a grammatical note that does not affect the logical concept but shows how the term is derived.
This step is for completeness; it is not essential for the logical definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ كَثُرَ مَعْنَاهُ) عطف على قوله : «إنِ اتَّحد» ; أي: إن كثر معنى المفرد، فلا يخلو من أن يكون المفرد موضوعاً لكل من المعاني الكثيرة أو لا . (فَإِنْ وُضِعَ) المفرد (لِكُلٍّ) من المعاني الكثيرة؛
(قَوْلُهُ : فَإِنْ وُضِعَ لِكُلِّ مِنَ الْمَعَانِي) أي : فإِنْ وُضِعَ لكلِّ واحدٍ منها بوضع شخصي، وليس المراد الأعم من الشَّخصيّ والنَّوعي، وإلا لم يصح .
(قَوْلُهُ : فَمُشْتَرَكُ) كالعين.
(قَوْلُهُ: فَمُشْتَرَكُ) الأصل : فمشتراً فيه، فَحُذِفَ الجار، واتَّصلَ الضَّمير بالوصف، فهو مِنَ الحذف والإيصال.
(And if its meaning is numerous) is connected to His statement: 'if it is unified'; meaning: if the meaning of the single word is numerous, then it is inevitable that the single word is either placed for each of the numerous meanings or not. (Then if it is placed) the single word (for each) of the numerous meanings; (His statement: Then if it is placed for each of the meanings) i.e., if it is placed for each one of them by an individual placement, and what is meant is not the more general than the individual and generic, otherwise it would not be valid. (His statement: Then it is mushtarak) like 'ayn. (His statement: then mushtarak) the original is mushtarak fihi (shared in it), so the preposition was deleted and the pronoun was attached to the description, so it is from deletion and attachment.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'mushtarak' (homonym/equivocal) and specifies the condition for a word to be considered mushtarak. The author begins by explaining that when a single word has multiple meanings, it must be determined whether it is placed (wudi'a) for all of those meanings or not. The key condition is that the word must be placed for each meaning by an individual placement (wad' shakhsi), meaning a separate act of linguistic stipulation for each meaning. The commentator clarifies that this is not the more general sense that includes generic placement (wad' naw'i), otherwise the definition would be invalid. The text then gives the classic example 'ayn (عين), which can mean eye, spring, spy, etc. The commentator also notes the linguistic form of the word 'mushtarak', explaining that it originates from 'mushtarak fihi' with the preposition deleted and the pronoun attached, a common pattern in Arabic. This definition distinguishes mushtarak from mutawati' (univocal) and mushakkik (analogical), which are discussed elsewhere. The condition of individual placement ensures that words used in multiple senses metaphorically (majaz) are not considered true mushtarak.

وضع شخصي

Checkpoint
Why is the word 'salat' (صلاة) not considered mushtarak between its linguistic meaning (du'a) and its legal meaning (ritual prayer)?
A) Because it has only one meaning.
B) Because it is placed for the legal meaning by a separate individual placement.
C) Because the legal meaning is a metaphorical extension from the linguistic meaning, not a separate placement.
D) Because it is mutawati'.

Key Takeaway
Mushtarak is defined by separate individual placement for each meaning, not mere multiplicity of meanings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine the word 'nisab' (نصاب) which means both 'threshold for zakat' and 'group of people'. Is it mushtarak according to the definition? Explain using the condition of wad' shakhsi.
?
The word 'mawt' (موت) in Arabic means 'death' and also 'stagnation (of water)'. Are both meanings individually placed? Determine if 'mawt' qualifies as mushtarak and justify your answer.

Distinguishing Manqul, Majaz, and Mushtarak
Commentator defines manqul, majaz, mushtarak lafzi, and mushtarak ma'nawi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish between manqul, majaz, mushtarak lafzi, and mushtarak ma'nawi based on the criteria of initial wad', prevalence, and preservation of original meaning.

Core Idea
Classification of lexical meanings depends on wad' history and usage, not just multiple meanings.

Words can be classified by how their meanings are assigned. This lesson unpacks three categories: manqul (transferred), majaz (metaphorical), and mushtarak (equivocal). The commentator provides precise criteria to distinguish them, a crucial tool for interpreting Islamic legal and theological texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base categories
The passage starts from the condition 'otherwise (wa-illa)', meaning a word not posited for each meaning individually. It then branches into manqul (usage predominant in second meaning) and majaz (usage not predominant).
Manqul: original meaning abandoned; majaz: original meaning remains.
2
Understand the criteria for manqul vs. majaz
Manqul requires that the transfer be well-known (mushtahir) to the extent that the original meaning is no longer commonly used. Majaz retains the original meaning and needs a contextual indicator (qarina).
Example: 'salah' from du'a to ritual prayer is manqul; 'asad' for a brave man is majaz.
3
Distinguish mushtarak lafzi and ma'nawi
Mushtarak lafzi: a single word posited for multiple distinct meanings (e.g., 'ayn). Mushtarak ma'nawi: a universal meaning applying to particulars like mutawati' and mushakkik – the sharing is in the meaning, not the word.
Commentator stresses that describing the word as mutawati' or mushakkik is actually describing the signifier by the signified's individuals.
4
Apply the classification to a concrete example
Take the word 'wudu'. In language, it means 'cleanliness'; in Sharia, it is a specific ritual act. Is it manqul or majaz? Use the criteria: the shar'i meaning is predominant in fiqh, and the linguistic meaning is rarely intended, so it is manqul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(قَوْلُهُ : بَلْ وُضِعَ لِمَعْنًى) أي : وضعاً شخصيًّا، وقوله: (ثم استعمل في معنى آخر لمناسبة)؛ أي: من غير وضع له (١) ، ولا يُقالُ : هذا يفيد أنَّ المنقول والمجاز العطار الخلخالي : فإن قلت: إذا كانَ اللَّفْظُ موضوعاً لثلاث معانٍ مثلاً، وكان وضعُهُ لاثنين منها ابتداءً دونَ الثَّالِثِ؛ فهذا اللَّفْظُ هَل هو مُشترك أم لا؟ ؛ قلتُ : الظَّاهِرُ بناءً على هذا القيد يعني الوضع الابتدائي المفشر بما سبق أنَّهُ ليس بمشترك ؛ إذ لم يوضع لِكُلِّ تلك المعاني ابتداء، لكنَّ التَّحقيقَ يَقتضي أنْ يكونَ مُشتركاً بالنسبة إلى المعنين هو موضوع لهما ابتداء، ومنقولاً بالنسبة إلى المعنى الثَّالث، فإنَّ امتياز الأقسام في هذا التقسيم لِكَوْنِهِ اعتباريًا ؛ إنَّما هو باعتبار الحيثيات والاعتبارات . (قَوْلُهُ : لِمُنَاسَبَةٍ) أي : بين المعنيين. اللَّذَيْنِ (۱) (قَوْلُهُ : من غير وضع له المنفي الوضع التحقيقي ، فلا ينافي أن المجاز والمنقول موضوعان أيضاً لكن بالوضع التأويلي وهو ما احتيج فيه إلى قرينة، وبهذا يجاب عن الاعتراض الذي ذكره بقوله : ولا يقال ... إلخ . ا.هـ . الشَّرنوبي .
And otherwise (i.e., if it was not posited for each of the meanings, but was posited for a meaning, then used in another meaning due to some connection), then it is either that its usage is well-known in the second meaning more than the first, or not. Al-Dasuqi: the descriptor characterized by univocality, i.e., agreement, and analogical, and sharing: the individuals, not the single word, upon the universal concept. Therefore, describing the single word with what was mentioned is describing the signifier by a description of the individuals of the signified. The signifier. (His statement: 'mushtarak') i.e., verbal, relative to the word, because of the sharing of meanings in the word posited for them, and includes 'al-'ilm' (knowledge) if multiple. As for the conceptual mushtarak (al-mushtarak al-ma'nawi), it is the universal meaning that applies to particulars, as in the univocal and the analogical, so the meaning of the word in them is shared in meaning, because of the sharing of individuals in the meaning for which the word is posited. (His statement: 'Rather, it was posited for a meaning') i.e., a personal positing. And his statement: 'then used in another meaning due to a connection' i.e., without a positing for it. And it cannot be said: this implies that the transferred (manqul) and the metaphorical (majaz) [are not posited]. Al-'Attar: Al-Khalkhali said: If you say: if a word is posited for three meanings, for instance, and its positing for two of them is initially without the third, then is this word mushtarak or not? I say: the apparent based on this condition, meaning the initial positing as explained previously, is that it is not mushtarak, because it was not posited for all those meanings initially. But verification requires that it be mushtarak with respect to the two meanings for which it is initially posited, and manqul with respect to the third meaning, for the distinction of the sections in this division, due to its being relative, is only according to aspects and considerations. (His statement: 'for a connection') i.e., between the two meanings. The two. (1) (His statement: 'without a positing for it' – the negation is of the real wad' (al-wad' al-tahqiqi), so it does not contradict that the majaz and the manqul are also posited, but by the interpretive wad' (al-wad' al-ta'wili), which is what requires a contextual indicator, and with this the objection that he mentioned is answered by his statement: 'and it cannot be said... etc.' End quote. Al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) that completes the classification of words beyond mushtarak. It defines the remaining categories: manqul (transferred term) and majaz (metaphorical term). The commentator begins with the condition 'otherwise' (wa-illa), which refers to a word that was not posited for each meaning individually but was originally posited for one meaning and then used for another due to some connection (munasaba). Such usage may become predominant (mushtahir) in the second meaning or not, leading to the distinction between manqul (if the transfer is well-known and the original meaning is abandoned) and majaz (if the original meaning remains). The commentator then clarifies that describing a word as mutawati', mushakkik, or mushtarak is really describing the signifier (the word) by a description that applies to the individuals of the signified (the meanings). He provides an important distinction between mushtarak lafzi (verbal equivocation) and mushtarak ma'nawi (conceptual equivocation, i.e., a universal meaning applying to particulars). The core of the passage is a question by al-Khalkhali: what if a word is posited for three meanings, but only two are by initial wad' and the third comes later? The answer is that such a word is mushtarak relative to the two initial meanings and manqul relative to the third meaning, because the classification is relative (i'tibari). This highlights the nuanced approach: the same word can belong to different categories depending on the aspect considered. Finally, a footnote by al-Sharnubi clarifies that 'without a wad' for it' means without a real wad' (al-wad' al-tahqiqi), but manqul and majaz do have an interpretive wad' (al-wad' al-ta'wili) that depends on a contextual indicator (qarina). This answers the potential objection that manqul and majaz would be unregulated.

Key Terms
منقول
مجاز
مشترك لفظي
مشترك معنوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best describes a manqul (transferred term) according to the passage?
A) A word used for a second meaning while the original meaning is still predominant.
B) A word posited for multiple distinct meanings from the beginning.
C) A word whose usage in a second meaning becomes so common that the original meaning is effectively abandoned.
D) A word that requires a contextual indicator (qarina) to be understood ina second meaning.

Key Takeaway
Manqul and majaz are distinguished by whether the original meaning is abandoned; mushtarak is distinguished by initial wad' for multiple meanings, with lafzi and ma'nawi subtypes based on whether the sharing is in the word or the meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Give an example of mushtarak ma'nawi from the science of Kalam (e.g., 'wujud' as a universal meaning applying to Allah and creatures) and explain why it is not mushtarak lafzi.

The Relative Nature of Lexical Classification: Al-Khalkhali's Question
Al-Khalkhali's question on a word with mixed wad' and usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of relative classification (i'tibari) to determine whether a word with multiple meanings is mushtarak or manqul depending on the aspect considered.

Core Idea
A single word can be both mushtarak and manqul from different perspectives.

Al-Khalkhali raises a subtle question: what if a word has two meanings from initial wad' and a third meaning from later usage? The commentator's answer reveals that classification is relative (i'tibari) and depends on which meaning we focus. This is a powerful tool for nuanced analysis of Islamic legal and theological terminology.

Guided Path
1
Understand al-Khalkhali's scenario
Suppose a word is initially posited (wad') for two meanings. Later, it is used for a third meaning without initial wad'. The question: is the word mushtarak (equivocal) or not?
The two initial meanings have wad', the third does not.
2
Identify the apparent answer
At first glance, if 'mushtarak' requires wad' for each meaning, the word is not mushtarak because the third meaning lacks initial wad'. So it might seem not mushtarak at all.
The passage says 'الظاهر ... أنه ليس بمشترك'.
3
Apply the verificatory answer (tahqiq)
Upon verification, the word is mushtarak for the two initial meanings (since they have wad') and manqul for the third meaning (since it came by transfer). So it is both mushtarak and manqul depending on which meaning is considered.
The classification is relative (i'tibari) – it depends on the aspect (haythiyya).
4
Generalize the principle
The same word can belong to different categories simultaneously. This prevents rigid pigeonholing and allows a single word to have mixed status, as often happens in Islamic technical vocabulary.
Example: 'fiqh' originally meant 'understanding', then was used for 'jurisprudence' – it could be mushtarak (if both are common) or manqul (if the original is less common).

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) that completes the classification of words beyond mushtarak. It defines the remaining categories: manqul (transferred term) and majaz (metaphorical term). The commentator begins with the condition 'otherwise' (wa-illa), which refers to a word that was not posited for each meaning individually but was originally posited for one meaning and then used for another due to some connection (munasaba). Such usage may become predominant (mushtahir) in the second meaning or not, leading to the distinction between manqul (if the transfer is well-known and the original meaning is abandoned) and majaz (if the original meaning remains). The commentator then clarifies that describing a word as mutawati', mushakkik, or mushtarak is really describing the signifier (the word) by a description that applies to the individuals of the signified (the meanings). He provides an important distinction between mushtarak lafzi (verbal equivocation) and mushtarak ma'nawi (conceptual equivocation, i.e., a universal meaning applying to particulars). The core of the passage is a question by al-Khalkhali: what if a word is posited for three meanings, but only two are by initial wad' and the third comes later? The answer is that such a word is mushtarak relative to the two initial meanings and manqul relative to the third meaning, because the classification is relative (i'tibari). This highlights the nuanced approach: the same word can belong to different categories depending on the aspect considered. Finally, a footnote by al-Sharnubi clarifies that 'without a wad' for it' means without a real wad' (al-wad' al-tahqiqi), but manqul and majaz do have an interpretive wad' (al-wad' al-ta'wili) that depends on a contextual indicator (qarina). This answers the potential objection that manqul and majaz would be unregulated.

According to the passage, why is a word posited for two meanings initially and used for a third later considered both mushtarak and manqul?
A) Because the word has three meanings, so it must be mushtarak; the but later usage makes it manqul.
B) Because the classification is relative (i'tibari): for the two initial meanings it is mushtarak, for the third it is manqul.

Key Takeaway

Definition of Manqul and Its Conditions
Author defines manqul and distinguishes it from majaz.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions that make a word manqul and distinguish it from majaz using the criteria of abandonment of the first meaning.

Core Idea
Manqul is a word whose original meaning is abandoned for a new dominant one.

In this passage, the author defines manqul as a word that was originally for one meaning but became famous in a second meaning to the point that the first is no longer used. This is distinct from majaz, where the original meaning remains in use. The commentary also clarifies how to classify manqul based on its transferrer (Sharia, custom, or technical usage). Let's work through the conditions and apply them to Islamic terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its contrast
The passage opens by contrasting manqul with mushtarak (a word placed for multiple meanings) and with cases where the word is not transferred. Focus on the conditional: if a word is not placed for each meaning (i.e., not mushtarak) but is used in a second meaning due to a connection, then it may be manqul or majaz.
The key contrast is between manqul (where the first meaning is abandoned) and majaz (where it is not).
2
Extract the conditions for manqul
The text states two conditions: (1) the word becomes famous (ishtahara) in the second meaning, and (2) its use in the first meaning is abandoned (turk). These conditions must both be met for a word to be manqul.
Fame here means common usage, not mere occurrence.
3
Determine the classification based on the transferrer
The passage divides manqul into three types depending on who transfers it: shar'i (Islamic law), 'urfi (customary), and istilahi (technical). The commentary notes that attribution is actually to the domain (Sharia, custom, technical usage) rather than to the people themselves.
For example, 'salat' is transferred by the people of Sharia, so it is manqul shar'i.
4
Refine the understanding of attribution
The commentary clarifies that the phrase 'attributed to the one who transfers' is imprecise; it should be attributed to the domain of the transferrer. This refinement avoids confusion about the source of the new meaning.
The transferrer (naqil) is the group of people, but the transferred meaning belongs to the discipline (Shar'a, 'urf, istilah).
5
Apply the definition to an example
Consider the word 'salat': in language it meant 'supplication', but in Islamic law it became famous for the ritual prayer, and the linguistic meaning is no longer commonly used. This fulfils both conditions, so it is manqul shar'i.
If the linguistic meaning were still in common use, it would be majaz, not manqul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِلَّا )؛ أي: وإن لم يوضع لكل من المعاني، بل وضع لمعنى، ثم استعمل في معنى آخر لمناسبة؛ فلا يخلو من أن يكون استعماله مشتهراً في المعنى الثاني دون الأول أو لا (فإن اشتهر في المعنى الثاني وترك استعماله في الأول؛ فمنقول ينسب إلى الناقل). (قوله: فإن اشتهر) أي: استعمل في المعنى الثاني، وقوله: (وترك)؛ أي: عند الناقل استعماله في المعنى الأول. (قوله: ينسب إلى الناقل إشارة إلى تقسيم المنقول إلى؛ الشرعي: إن كان الناقل للفظ أهل الشرع، والعرفي: إن كان الناقل أهل عرف عام، والاصطلاحي: إن كان الناقل أهل اصطلاح وعرف خاص، وفيه نظر، وذلك لأن النسبة ليست إلى الناقل، بل إلى ما عليه الناقل من الشرع والعرف والاصطلاح. (قوله: فمنقول) وهو ما غلب في معنى مجازي للموضوع له الأول؛ حتى هجر الأول، فهو في اللغة حقيقة في المعنى الأول؛ مجاز في الثاني، وفي الاصطلاح المنقول إليه بالعكس كلَفْظِ الصلاة. (قوله: ينسب إلى الناقل لا يخفى أن الناقل حقيقة هو أهل الشرع أو الاصطلاح... إلخ؛ والمنقول إليه هو الشرع نفسه والاصطلاح؛ أي: ما اصطلحوا عليه).
And if not [placed for each meaning]; i.e., if it was not placed for each of the meanings, but rather placed for one meaning, then used in another meaning due to a connection; then it must be that its use is famous in the second meaning to the exclusion of the first, or not. If it becomes famous in the second meaning and its use in the first is abandoned, then it is manqul, attributed to the one who transfers. (His saying: 'if it becomes famous') i.e., it is used in the second meaning, and his saying: 'and its use is abandoned'; i.e., by the one who transfers, its use in the first meaning. (His saying: 'attributed to the one who transfers' refers to the division of manqul into: shar'i if the transferrer is the people of Sharia; 'urfi if the transferrer is people of general custom; istilahi if the transferrer is people of specific technical custom. There is consideration in this, because the attribution is not to the transferrer but to what the transferrer belongs to: Sharia, custom, or technical usage. (His saying: 'so it is manqul') it is that which predominates in a metaphorical meaning over the original meaning such that the original is abandoned; so in language it is a reality in the first meaning and a metaphor in the second, but in the transferred technical sense it is the opposite, like the word 'prayer'. (His saying: 'attributed to the one who transfers' it is not hidden that the transferrer in reality is the people of Sharia or technical usage... etc.; and the transferred-to is Sharia itself or technical usage; i.e., what they have agreed upon).
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of 'manqul' (the transferred term) and distinguishes it from 'majaz' (metaphor). It opens with a conditional: if a word is not placed for each of multiple meanings individually (i.e., it is not mushtarak), but rather was originally placed for a single meaning and then used in another meaning due to a connection (munasaba), then there are two possibilities. The word either becomes famous in the second meaning to the extent that the original is abandoned, or it does not. If it becomes famous and the original is abandoned, it is manqul. The attribution 'to the one who transfers' divides manqul into three types based on who the transferrer is: shar'i (Islamic law), 'urfi (customary), or istilahi (technical). However, the commentator notes an issue: the attribution is not to the transferrer themselves but to the domain they belong to, e.g., Sharia, custom, or technical usage. The passage then clarifies that manqul is essentially a word that is originally a metaphor but becomes so dominant in that metaphorical sense that it becomes a reality in that new sense, while the original meaning is abandoned. An example is the word 'salat' (prayer), which originally meant 'supplication' (du'a) in language but in Islamic law was transferred to the specific ritual prayer, to the extent that the linguistic meaning is no longer the primary understood sense. The passage thus provides a precise definition of manqul, distinguishing it from majaz which does not involve complete abandonment of the original meaning.

Key Terms
منقول (manqūl)
مجاز (majāz)
ناقل (nāqil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) Manqul is a type of mushtarak, while majaz is not.

Key Takeaway
Manqul is defined by two conditions: fame in the new meaning and abandonment of the old, distinguishing it from majaz.

Definition of Manqool and Its Three Types
Author defines manqool, explains its three types, and addresses an objection about attribution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a term to be considered manqool and classify it into one of the three types (shar'i, 'urfi, istilahi) based on the transferor.

Core Idea
A transferred term abandons its original meaning after becoming dominant in a metaphorical sense.

In this passage, the author defines a key concept in the logic of terms: the transferred term (manqool). Understanding this distinction is crucial for interpreting Islamic legal and theological texts, where many terms have shifted from their literal meanings to technical ones. We'll see the exact conditions and the threefold classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions for manqool
Look for the phrase 'if it becomes famous in the second meaning and its usage in the first is abandoned'. This establishes the term must be predominantly used in a metaphorical sense and the original literal meaning is no longer used.
2
Understand the classification based on the transferor
The text then says 'attributed to the transferor' and explains that if the transferor is the Lawgiver, it's shar'i; if general custom, 'urfi; if specific convention, istilahi.
The transferor is not necessarily a single person but the authority initiating the shift.
3
Analyze the objection about attribution
al-Dasuqi notes that the attribution should be to what the transferor is upon, not to the transferor himself. This is resolved by assuming an omitted genitive: 'attributed to what the transferor is upon'.
This nuance refines how we think about the classification.
4
Apply the classification to examples
The passage gives examples: 'salah' and 'sawm' are shar'i manqool. Think of other examples from Islamic law or conventions to test your understanding.
For instance, 'zakat' originally meant 'growth' but was transferred to obligatory charity.

فصل: في مباحث الألفاظ
(فإن اشتهر في المعنى الثاني وترك استعماله في الأول؛ فمنقول ينسب إلى الناقل).
قال الدسوقي: (قوله: فإن اشتهر) أي: استعمل في المعنى الثاني، وقوله: (وترك)؛ أي: عند الناقل استعماله في المعنى الأول. (قوله: ينسب إلى الناقل) إشارة إلى تقسيم المنقول إلى؛ الشرعي: إن كان الناقل للفظ أهل الشرع، والعرفي: إن كان الناقل أهل عرف عام، والاصطلاحي: إن كان الناقل أهل اصطلاح وعرف خاص، وفيه نظر، وذلك لأن النسبة ليست إلى الناقل، بل إلى ما عليه الناقل من الشرع والعرف والاصطلاح.
وقال العطار: (قوله: فمنقول) وهو ما غلب في معنى مجازي للموضوع له الأول؛ حتى هجر الأول، فهو في اللغة حقيقة في المعنى الأول؛ مجاز في الثاني، وفي الاصطلاح المنقول إليه بالعكس كلفظ الصلاة. (قوله: ينسب إلى الناقل) لا يخفى أن الناقل حقيقة هو أهل الشرع أو الاصطلاح... والمنقول إليه هو الشرع نفسه والاصطلاح...
فإن كان الناقل شرعاً؛ فمنقول شرعي، كالصلاة والصوم.
وأجيب: بأن الكلام على حذف مضاف؛ أي: ينسب ذلك المنقول إلى ما عليه الناقل.
وفيه: أنه لا ينسب إلى ما عليه الناقل مطلقاً، حتى يقال: نحوي أو منطقي، بل على الوجوه الثلاثة المذكورة.
والمفهوم من قوله: (ينسب إلى الناقل)؛ أعم من التقسيم المشار إليه. وأجيب: بأن كيفية النسبة مشهورة، فاعتمد على اشتهارها.
(قوله: شرعاً) أي: ذا شرع أو شارعاً. (قوله: كالصلاة) أي: فإنها لغة: الدعاء، نقلها الشارع للعبادة المعلومة؛ لاشتمالها على الدعاء. (قوله: والصوم) أي: فإنه لغة: الإمساك مطلقاً، نقله الشارع إلى الإمساك من طلوع الفجر للغروب عن شهوات البطن والفرج، وما يقوم مقامهما.
Section: On topics of terms. (If it becomes famous in the second meaning and its usage in the first is abandoned, then it is a transferred term (manqool) attributed to the transferor.) al-Dasuqi said: His saying 'if it becomes famous' means it is used in the second meaning, and his saying 'and abandoned' means the transferor abandoned its use in the first meaning. His saying 'attributed to the transferor' indicates the division of the transferred term into: shar'i, if the transferors of the term are the people of sacred law; 'urfi, if the transferors are people of general custom; and istilahi, if the transferors are people of convention and specific custom. But there is consideration in this, because the attribution is not to the transferor, but to what the transferor is upon of sacred law, custom, or convention. al-'Attar said: His saying 'so it is a transferred term' refers to that which prevails in a metaphorical meaning for the original literal sense until the original is abandoned; so in language it is literal in the first meaning and metaphorical in the second, and in the transferred-to convention it is the opposite, like the word 'salah'. His saying 'attributed to the transferor' – it is not hidden that the transferor in reality is the people of sacred law or convention... and the transferred-to is the sacred law itself and the convention... So if the transferor is the Lawgiver, it is a shar'i transferred term, like 'salah' and 'sawm'. It was answered that the expression involves an omitted genitive; that is, that transferred term is attributed to what the transferor is upon. Still, it is not attributed absolutely to what the transferor is upon, such that it would be said 'grammatical' or 'logical', but only according to the three mentioned ways. The understood meaning from his saying 'attributed to the transferor' is more general than the indicated division. And it was answered that the manner of attribution is well-known, so it relies on its fame. His saying 'shar'an' means 'possessor of a law' or 'lawgiver'. His saying 'like salah' means it is language: supplication; the Lawgiver transferred it to the known act of worship because it includes supplication. His saying 'and sawm' means it is language: absolute abstention; the Lawgiver transferred it to abstention from desires of the stomach and private parts from dawn to sunset, and what takes their place.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage defines the transferred term (manqool) in the context of the logic of terms. It begins with the matn statement: if a term becomes famous in a secondary metaphorical meaning while its original literal meaning is abandoned, it is called manqool and is attributed to the transferor (naqil). The commentators then expand on this definition. al-Dasuqi explains that the condition of being 'famous' means it is commonly used in the second meaning, and the abandonment is from the perspective of the transferor, not necessarily all speakers. He then points out that the phrase 'attributed to the transferor' hints at the classification of manqool into three types: (1) shar'i, when the transferor is the Lawgiver (shar'); (2) 'urfi, when the transferor is general custom; (3) istilahi, when the transferor is a specific convention. However, al-Dasuqi objects that the attribution should actually be to what the transferor is upon (i.e., the law, custom, or convention), not to the transferor himself. al-'Attar then provides a more precise definition: manqool is a term that becomes dominant in a metaphorical sense such that the original literal meaning falls into disuse. He gives the example of 'salah', which originally meant 'supplication' but was transferred by the Lawgiver to the specific ritual prayer due to its inclusion of supplication. He also gives the example of 'sawm', from 'absolute abstention' to 'fasting from dawn to sunset with the intention of worship'. The passage then addresses an objection: if the attribution were to the transferor, why not say 'grammatical' or 'logical'? The answer is that the object is omitted—the attribution is to what the transferor is upon, i.e., the three mentioned categories. Finally, it is noted that the expression 'attributed to the transferor' is broader than the threefold division, but the specific three are well-known, so it is acceptable to rely on that fame.

Three Types of Manqul: Shar'i, Ishtilahi, 'Urfi
Author classifies manqul into three types based on the nature of the naqil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify any given manqul term as shar'i, ishtilahi, or 'urfi based on its transmitter (naqil) and explain the attribution nuance with the concept of hadhf mudaf.

Core Idea
A term's meaning can be transferred by religious law, technical experts, or common custom – each yields a distinct type of manqul.

This lesson explores the classification of transferred terms (manqul) in the Islamic intellectual tradition. Understanding this classification is crucial for properly interpreting terms in usul al-fiqh, grammar, and other sciences. We will examine how the nature of the transmitter (naqil) determines whether a term is shar'i (religious), ishtilahi (technical), or 'urfi (customary), and clarify a subtle point about attribution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types and their transmitters
Find in the text the three categories: shar'i (transmitter = legislator), ishtilahi (transmitter = specialists like grammarians), 'urfi (transmitter = common custom). Note each definition and example.
The transmitter is the entity that transfers the meaning.
2
Analyze the examples for each type
For each type, note the original linguistic meaning and the new transferred meaning. For example, 'salat' originally meant supplication, now means ritual prayer. Check how the transfer relates to the transmitter's domain.
3
Understand the attribution controversy
The text discusses whether the transferred term is attributed directly to the transmitter or to the transmitter's domain. The solution involves 'hadhf mudaf' (omitted genitive). Grasp the nuance: we say 'shar'i' meaning 'related to the Lawgiver' but actually it's 'attributed to the domain of the Lawgiver'.
This nuance prevents confusion with fields like grammar (nahwi) or logic (mantiqi).
4
Apply the classification to a new example
Try to classify a term like 'wudu' (ritual purity) – is it shar'i? Yes, because the Lawgiver transferred its meaning from 'cleanliness' to a specific ritual act. Identify the original and transferred meanings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كانَ النَّاقل شرعاً؛ فمنقول شرعي، كالصَّلاة والصوم. وأجيبُ: بأنَّ الكلامَ على حذف مضاف؛ أي: ينسب ذلك المنقولُ إلى ما عليه الناقل. وفيهِ: أَنَّهُ لا يُنسب إلى ما عليهِ النَّاقلُ مُطلقاً، حتّى يُقال: نحوي أو منطقي، بل على الوجوهِ الثَّلاثةِ المذكورة. والمفهومُ مِن قولِهِ: (يُنسب إلى النَّاقل)؛ أعمُ مِنَ التَّقسيم المشار إليه. وأُجيبُ: بأنَّ كيفيَّةَ النّسبة مشهورة، فاعتمد على اشتهارِهَا. (قَوْلُهُ: شَرْعاً) أي: ذا شرع أو شارعاً. (قَوْلُهُ: كَالصَّلَاةِ) أي: فإنَّها لغةً: الدُّعاءُ، نقلها الشارع للعبادة المعلومة؛ لاشتمالها على الدُّعاء. (قَوْلُهُ: وَالصَّوْمِ) أي: فإِنَّهُ لغةً: الإمساك مُطلقاً، نقلَهُ الشَّارِعُ إلى الإمساكِ مِن طلوع الفجر للغروب عن شهوات البطن والفرج، وَمَا يقومُ مَقامَهما. (قَوْلُهُ: وَإِنْ كَانَ اصْطِلَاحَاً) أي: أهلَ اصطلاحِ وعُرف خاص، وأفردَ الشَّرعيَّ عَنْ غيرِهِ وإِنْ كانَ مِنَ الاصطلاحيَّاتِ؛ لِشَرَفِه. (قَوْلُهُ: كَالْفَاعِل) أي: فإنَّهُ في اللُّغةِ: مَنْ أوجد الفعل؛ أي: الحدث، ثمَّ نقله النُّحاةُ إلى الاسم المرفوع الذي أسند له فِعْل أو شبهه على جهة قيامه به، أو وقوعه منه. (قَوْلُهُ: وَالْمَفْعُوْلِ) أي: فإِنَّهُ لغةٌ: مَن وقعَ عليهِ الفِعْل، ثمَّ نقلَهُ النُّحاةُ إلى الاسم المنصوب بالفعل وشبهه. (قَوْلُهُ: وَإِنْ كَانَ عُرْفَاً) أي: وإِنْ كانَ النَّاقلُ عُرفاً؛ أي: أهلَ عُرفِ عام؛ بأن كانُوا غير مُعيَّنين. (قَوْلُهُ: كَالدَّابَّةِ) أي: فإنَّها لغةٌ: كلُّ ما دبَّ على الأرض؛ آدميا أو غيره، فنُقِلَ في عُرْفِ النَّاسِ لِذَاتِ القوائم الأربع، فقولُهُ: (لِذَاتِ القوائم الأربع)؛ أي: المنقولة من معناها اللُّغويّ لِذَاتِ القوائم الأربع.
If the transmitter is religious law, then it is a religiously transferred term, like prayer and fasting. And it was answered: that the statement involves an omitted genitive construction; that is: that transferred term is attributed to what the transmitter is based upon. And in it: that it is not attributed to what the transmitter is based upon absolutely, such that it is said: grammatical or logical, but rather according to the three mentioned aspects. And what is understood from his statement 'attributed to the transmitter' is more general than the indicated division. And it was answered: that the manner of attribution is well-known, so he relied on its being well-known. (His statement: 'religiously') that is: possessing a law or a legislator. (His statement: 'like prayer') that is: it is in language supplication; the legislator transferred it to the known worship because it includes supplication. (His statement: 'and fasting') that is: it is in language absolute abstention; the legislator transferred it to abstention from dawn to sunset from desires of the belly and private parts and what takes their place. (His statement: 'and if it is technical') that is: people of a technical convention and a specific custom, and he singled out the religious from others even though it is among the technical conventions, due to its nobility. (His statement: 'like the agent') that is: it is in language the one who produces the action; i.e., the event; then the grammarians transferred it to the nominative noun to which a verb or its like is attributed in the manner of its being performed by it or occurring from it. (His statement: 'and the object') that is: it is in language the one upon whom the action falls; then the grammarians transferred it to the accusative noun by the verb and its like. (His statement: 'and if it is customary') that is: if the transmitter is custom; i.e., people of common custom; being unspecified individuals. (His statement: 'like al-dābbah') that is: it is in language everything that crawls on the earth; human or other; then it was transferred in people's custom to possessor of four legs; so his statement 'for possessor of four legs' means: transferred from its linguistic meaning to possessor of four legs.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the concept of 'manqūl' (transferred term) into three types according to the nature of the transmitter (nāqil). The author, drawing from earlier commentaries, explains each type with examples from Islamic sciences. The first type is sharʿī (religious) transfer, where the transmitter is the Lawgiver (Allah or the Prophet). Examples are 'ṣalāh' (prayer) and 'ṣawm' (fasting): originally linguistic terms meaning 'supplication' and 'abstention' respectively, they were transferred by the Lawgiver to specific ritual acts. The second type is iṣṭilāḥī (technical) transfer, where the transmitter is a specific group of specialists, like grammarians. Examples are 'fāʿil' (agent) and 'mafʿūl' (object): originally general linguistic terms, the grammarians transferred them to specific syntactic roles. The third type is ʿurfī (customary) transfer, where the transmitter is the common people (ʿurf ʿāmm). An example is 'dābbah', which originally meant any crawling creature, but in common custom came to refer specifically to four-legged animals like horses and donkeys. The passage also addresses a subtlety: the attribution of the transferred term is not simply to the transmitter, but to the transmitter's domain (e.g., sharʿ, naḥw). It notes that this classification covers only three of many possible logical divisions, but they are the only ones that actually occur. The commentary further clarifies each point, emphasizing the precision of the definitions.

Key Terms
المنقول
الناقل
حذف مضاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Salat (prayer)
B) Fa'il (agent)
C) Dabba (animal)

Key Takeaway
Manqul terms are classified by their transmitter: religious law, technical specialists, or common custom; each type has specific examples and attribution rules.

Types of Transmitted Words: Istilahi and 'Urfi
Classification of transmitted terms with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between istilahi and 'urfi transmitted terms and correctly classify examples from Islamic disciplines.

Core Idea
Words shift meaning by either expert convention or popular custom.

Not all words mean what they originally did. Language evolves through deliberate transfer (naql). This lesson unpacks two categories of naql: one by a specialized group (istilah), and one by general public use ('urf). You will learn to spot both using examples from grammar and common speech.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of transmission
Read the passage's opening line: it classifies introduced terms based on the agent of change — either a specific technical group or common people.
2
Analyse the istilahi example: al-fāʿil
The passage explains that in language fāʿil means 'one who does an act', but grammarians shifted it to mean the formal subject (nominative noun with verb). That shift is by a defined group.
The commentator notes that sharʿi terms are a noble subset of istilahi, not a separate type.
3
Analyse the 'urfi example: al-dābba
Originally 'any creeping thing', common custom narrowed it to four-legged animals. The transmitters are unspecified, i.e. general public usage.
4
Apply the distinction to new examples
Test yourself: is the term 'ṣalāt' (ritual prayer) istilahi or 'urfi? It's istilahi (sharʿi subset) because the Lawgiver defined it. If you classify an example, state whether the transmitting community is specific (like jurists) or general.

فَصْلٌ: في مباحثِ الألفاظ
وإن كان اصطلاحاً ؛ فمنقول اصطلاحاً، كالفاعل والمفعول.
وإن كان عُرفاً ؛ فعرفي كالدَّابَّةِ لِذَاتِ القوائم الأربع
(قَوْلُهُ : وَإِنْ كَانَ اصْطِلَاحَاً) أي : أهلَ اصطلاحِ وعُرف خاص، وأفردَ الشَّرعيَّ عَنْ غيرِهِ وإِنْ كانَ مِنَ الاصطلاحيَّاتِ ؛ لِشَرَفِه .
(قَوْلُهُ : كَالْفَاعِل أي : فإنَّهُ في اللُّغةِ : مَنْ أوجد الفعل؛ أي: الحدث، ثمَّ نقله النُّحاةُ إلى الاسم المرفوع الذي أسند له فِعْل أو شبهه على جهة قيامه به، أو وقوعه منه .
(قَوْلُهُ : وَالْمَفْعُوْلِ) أي : فإِنَّهُ لغةٌ : مَن وقعَ عليهِ الفِعْل، ثمَّ نقلَهُ النُّحاةُ إلى الاسم المنصوب بالفعل وشبهه . (قَوْلُهُ : وَإِنْ كَانَ عُرْفَاً ) أي : وإِنْ كانَ النَّاقلُ عُرفاً ؛ أي: أهلَ عُرفِ عام؛ بأن كانُوا غير مُعيَّنين . (قَوْلُهُ : كَالدَّابَّةِ) أي : فإنَّها لغةٌ : كلُّ ما دبَّ على الأرض؛ آدميا أو غيره، فنُقِلَ في عُرْفِ النَّاسِ لِذَاتِ القوائم الأربع، فقولُهُ: (لِذَاتِ القوائم الأربع)؛ أي : المنقولة من معناها اللُّغويّ لِذَاتِ القوائم الأربع.
Section: On Investigations of Terms. If the transmission is by convention (istilah), then it is a technical transferred term, like 'al-fāʿil' (the agent) and 'al-mafʿūl' (the object). If it is by custom ('urf), then it is a customary transferred term, like 'al-dābba' (beast) for the four-legged animal. (His statement: 'If it is by convention') meaning: the people of a specific convention and special custom; he singled out the religious term from others even though it is also from conventional terms, due to its honor. (His statement: 'like al-fāʿil') meaning: in language it refers to the one who performs the act, i.e., the event; then the grammarians transferred it to the nominative noun to which a verb or its equivalent is attributed in the manner of its being performed by it or occurring from it. (His statement: 'and al-mafʿūl') meaning: in language it is the one upon whom the act falls; then the grammarians transferred it to the accusative noun of the verb or its equivalent. (His statement: 'If it is by custom') meaning: the transmitter is custom, i.e., people of general custom, meaning they are not specified. (His statement: 'like al-dābba') meaning: in language it is everything that creeps on the earth, human or otherwise, then it was transferred in the custom of people to the four-legged animal. So his statement: 'for the four-legged' means the term transferred from its linguistic meaning to the four-legged.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies types of transmitted terms (manqūl). It is a definitional passage. The author begins by stating the section on investigations of terms. Then he presents the classification: if a word is transmitted from its original meaning by a specific group (like grammarians), it is an istilāḥī (conventional) term; examples are 'al-fāʿil' (agent) and 'al-mafʿūl' (object) which the grammarians repurposed for syntactic roles. If the transmission is by general custom ('urf), it is a 'urfī term; example: 'al-dābba' originally meant any creeping creature but in common custom came to mean four-legged animals. The commentary from al-Dasūqī explains that the author separated sharʿī (religious) terms from istilāḥī due to their honor, though they are also conventional. Al-ʿAṭṭār clarifies that customary transmission involves unspecified transmitters. The explanation uses the linguistic meanings before transmission. Thus, the passage establishes a taxonomy of transmitted terms based on the type of transmitting community.

Haqiqa and Majaz: Definitions, Example, and Logical Issues
Author defines haqiqa and majaz and addresses two logical issues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define haqiqa and majaz, apply the definition to a word like 'asad', and explain the two issues regarding the classification and 'Abd al-Hakim's resolution.

Core Idea
Haqiqa and majaz are defined by usage, not by a one-to-one correspondence.

This passage from al-'Attar's commentary on the Tadhhib al-Mantiq establishes the criteria for distinguishing literal (haqiqa) and figurative (majaz) word usage. Using the classic example of 'asad' (lion), the author then addresses two subtle issues that arise from this classification and provides a resolution. Mastering this distinction is critical for Islamic legal theory (usul al-fiqh) where determining the literal meaning is the default interpretive principle.

Guided Path
1
Locate the condition for haqiqa and majaz
Find the condition phrase: the word must have multiple meanings, it must not have become so famous in the second meaning that the first is abandoned, and its usage in the first meaning persists. The passage states: 'Otherwise (i.e., if it has not become famous in the second meaning and its usage in the first meaning has not been abandoned), then it is haqiqa if used in the first meaning... and majaz if used in the second.'
The condition ensures that haqiqa and majaz are defined relative to the word's history.
2
Apply the condition to 'asad'
The word 'asad' has two meanings: the animal (lion) and the brave man. When used for the animal, it is haqiqa; when used for the brave man, it is majaz. The original meaning (lion) is not abandoned and the second meaning (brave man) is not so famous as to replace the first.
3
Identify the first logical issue raised by Abū al-Fatḥ
Abū al-Fatḥ objects that listing haqiqa and majaz as divisions of a word with multiple meanings suggests that every haqiqa has a corresponding majaz, but that is not true. Many words have only a literal use.
This is a critique of the classification's implication.
4
Understand 'Abd al-Ḥakīm's response
'Abd al-Ḥakīm answers that the terms haqiqa and majaz are applied to the same word from two different perspectives (considerations of usage), not that every literal word necessarily has a metaphorical counterpart. The classification describes how a word can be used, not a property of the word itself.
This preserves the classification without the problematic implication.
5
Identify the second issue: the intermediate case
Abū al-Fatḥ notes that both haqiqa and majaz are conditioned by actual usage; a word before any usage is neither, creating an intermediate category. The passage does not resolve this explicitly, but it acknowledges the issue.
This raises the question of words that are never used in their assigned meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِلَّا ) أي : وإنْ لم يَشتَهِرُ في المعنى الثَّاني، ولم يُتْرَكِ استعماله في الأوَّلِ ؛ (فَحَقِيْقَةٌ) (۱) إن استعمل في المعنى الأوَّل، كـ: «الأسد» للحيوان المعلوم. [المجاز] (وَمَجَازٌ) إن استُعمِلَ في المعنى الثَّاني، كـ «الأسد» للرَّجل الشَّجاع . (قَوْلُهُ : فَحَقِيقَةٌ) أو مجاز .... ثمَّ المرادُ : أَنَّهُ وضع لكلِّ مِنَ المعاني مِن غيرِ ملاحظة مناسبة، وسواء كان الوضعُ للمعاني في زمنٍ واحدٍ أو أزمنةٍ مُتعدّدة.
(قَوْلُهُ : وَإِلَّا ، فَحَقِيقَةٌ وَمَجَازٌ) لا يتعيَّنُ أنْ يكونَ مَجازاً، بل يحتمل أن يكون كِنايةٌ، فَلَا بُدَّ أنْ يكونَ ذلك المجازُ هُنَا على سبيلِ التَّمثيل إلى حقيقة ومجاز أو كناية، أو المرادُ مِنَ المجاز أعمُّ مِنَ المجازِ والكنايةِ مَجازا، ويجوز أنْ يكونَ المجاز عند المنطقيين أعمَّ مِنْهُمَا مِن بَابِ تَخالُفِ الاصطلاحين . قال أبو الفتح : ثم هَهُنا بَحثانِ ؛ الأَوَّلُ : أَنَّ عَدَّ الحقيقة والمجازِ مِن أقسام اللَّفْظِ الَّذِي تَعدَّدَ مَعناهُ؛ يُشعِرُ بأنَّ الحقيقةَ مِمَّا يتعدَّدُ مَعناه، وأنَّ لِكُلِّ حقيقةٍ مَجازاً، وليس كذلك، وأما أنَّ كُلَّ مَجاز له حقيقةٌ ، فَنَعَمْ، فَالإشكالُ بالنِّسبة للحقيقة، الثَّاني : أَنَّ كُلَّا مِنَ الحقيقةِ والمجاز مشروط بالاستعمال ؛ فاللَّفْظُ قبل الاستعمال ليس حقيقةً ولا مجازاً، فيبقى واسطةٌ بين الأقسام، وأجابَ عبد الحكيم عن الأوَّلِ؛ بأنَّ معنى قولهم : فَحقيقةٌ ومَجاز ؛ أي: يُسمَّى اللَّفْظُ المنقولُ باسمي الحقيقة والمجاز باعتبارَيْنِ، فَلَا يردُ أَنَّ
Otherwise (i.e., if it has not become famous in the second meaning and its usage in the first meaning has not been abandoned), then it is (ḥaqīqa) if used in the first meaning, like 'al-asad' for the known animal. And it is (majāz) if used in the second meaning, like 'al-asad' for the brave man. (His statement: 'faḥaqīqa') or majāz... then the intended meaning is that it is assigned to each of the meanings without regard to appropriateness, whether the assignment is in one time or multiple times. (His statement: 'Otherwise, faḥaqīqa wa majāz') it is not necessarily majāz; rather it could be kināya. So that majāz here must be by way of example of either ḥaqīqa and majāz or kināya, or the intended meaning of majāz is more general than majāz and kināza by way of figurativeness, and it is permissible that majāz with the logicians is more general than both, due to divergence of conventions. Abū al-Fatḥ said: There are two investigations here: First, counting ḥaqīqa and majāz among the divisions of a word that has multiple meanings suggests that ḥaqīqa also has multiple meanings and that every ḥaqīqa has a majāz, but that is not the case. As for every majāz having a ḥaqīqa, yes. So the problem is regarding ḥaqīqa. Second, both ḥaqīqa and majāz are conditioned by usage; the word before usage is neither ḥaqīqa nor majāz, so there remains an intermediate between the divisions. And ʿAbd al-Ḥakīm answered the first by saying that the meaning of their statement 'faḥaqīqa wa majāz' is that the transmitted word is called by the names ḥaqīqa and majāz according to two considerations, so the objection does not arise.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage defines ḥaqīqa and majāz and then addresses two logical issues. It opens with the condition: if a word with multiple meanings has not become so famous in the second meaning that the first is abandoned, then when used in the original meaning it is ḥaqīqa (literal), and when used in the secondary meaning it is majāz (metaphorical). The example is 'asad' for the lion (ḥaqīqa) and for the brave man (majāz). The commentary clarifies that the word is assigned to each meaning without regard to appropriateness, whether contemporaneously or separately. Then al-ʿAṭṭār notes that the term majāz here might actually include kināya (allusion), so it is used broadly; logicians may have a different convention. Then two issues are raised by Abū al-Fatḥ: (1) The classification implies every ḥaqīqa has a majāz, but that is false; (2) Usage is a condition, so before usage a word is neither, leaving an intermediate. ʿAbd al-Ḥakīm answers that the terms are applied to the word from two perspectives (one literal use, one metaphorical use), not that every literal word has a metaphorical counterpart. This preserves the classification without the problematic implication.

The Four Comprehensive Divisions of Nouns
Footnote enumerates the four comprehensive divisions of nouns with subcategories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any noun into one of the four divisions based on the relationship between its word-form and meaning.

Core Idea
Nouns are classified by whether word and meaning are one or many — four logical possibilities.

In this footnote, al-Sharnūbī completes the author's discussion by providing a comprehensive classification of nouns based on two criteria: the word (lafẓ) and the meaning (maʿnā). Understanding this classification is essential for logical analysis of terms in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two criteria
The classification uses two binary attributes: whether the word is one (muttaḥid) or multiple (mutaʿaddid), and whether the meaning is one or multiple. These yield four logical combinations.
2
Match each combination to the Arabic label
First: one word, one meaning — under it are ʿalam, mutawāṭiʾ, mushakkik. Second: one word, multiple meanings — under it are mushtarak, manqūl, ḥaqīqa, majāz. Third: multiple words, one meaning — this is synonymy (mushtarak in a different sense). Fourth: multiple words, multiple meanings — mutabāyin.
Note that 'mushtarak' appears in two divisions with different meanings.
3
Practice with examples from the passage
Take the example 'insān' (human). It has one word and one meaning — it belongs to the first division. 'ʿAyn' has one word and multiple meanings — second division. 'Ghaḍanfar' and 'hizabr' both mean lion — third division. 'Insān' and 'faras' are different words and meanings — fourth division.
Ensure you understand why each example fits.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن المصنف لم يستوعب أقسام الاسم، وإليك بيانها : هي أربعة إجمالاً وتسعة تفصيلاً، الأوَّل: ما اتَّحد لفظه ومعناه وتحته ثلاثة؛ العلم والمتواطئ والمشكك . الثَّاني : ما اتَّحد لفظه وتعدّد معناه وتحته أربعة؛ المشترك والمنقول والحقيقة والمجاز . الثَّالث : عكس الثَّاني، أي ما تعدّد لفظه واتَّحد معناه؛ وهو المشترك كغضنفر وهِزَبْر وقشورة للحيوان المفترس. الرَّابع عكس الأوّل ؛ أي: ما تعدّد لفظه ومعناه وهو المتباين كإنسان وفرس.
Know that the author did not exhaust the divisions of the noun. Here is their explanation: They are four in summary and nine in detail. First: what has unified word and meaning, and under it are three: the proper noun, the univocal, and the analogical. Second: what has unified word and multiple meanings, and under it are four: the shared term, the transmitted, the literal, and the metaphorical. Third: the opposite of the second, i.e., what has multiple words and unified meaning, and it is the synonymous, like 'ghaḍanfar', 'hizabr', and 'qashūra' for the predatory animal. Fourth: the opposite of the first, i.e., what has multiple words and multiple meanings, and it is the divergent, like 'insān' and 'faras'.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This footnote provides a comprehensive classification of nouns (asmā') based on the combination of word-form and meaning. It is an enumerative passage intended for memorization. The author (al-Sharnūbī) notes that the main text did not cover all divisions. There are four overarching categories: (1) single word and single meaning: includes proper nouns (ʿalam), univocal terms (mutawāṭi'), and analogical terms (mushakkik). (2) single word and multiple meanings: includes shared terms (mushtarak), transmitted terms (manqūl), literal (ḥaqīqa), and metaphorical (majāz). (3) multiple words and single meaning: this is synonymy (mushtarak – note: here mushtarak is used differently; it means synonymous, not homonymous), e.g., different words for lion. (4) multiple words and multiple meanings: these are divergent terms (mutabāyin), e.g., 'insān' and 'faras'. This classification is foundational for understanding logical and linguistic analysis of terms.

Key Takeaway

First Division: ʿAlam, Mutawāṭiʾ, and Mushakkik
Footnote enumerates the first division of nouns with three subtypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a proper noun (ʿalam), a univocal term (mutawāṭiʾ), and an analogical term (mushakkik).

Core Idea
Even when a word has one meaning, the way it applies varies — naming one individual, all equally, or with degrees.

The first division of nouns covers terms that have a single word and a single meaning, but differ in how they designate their referents. Al-Sharnūbī lists three subtypes: ʿalam, mutawāṭiʾ, and mushakkik. Mastering these distinctions is crucial for precise definition in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Recall the first division definition
First division: one word, one meaning (muttaḥid al-lafẓ wal-maʿnā). All three subtypes share this property.
2
Learn the three subtypes
ʿalam: a proper noun that names a specific entity (e.g., 'Zayd'). Mutawāṭiʾ: a univocal term that applies equally to all individuals (e.g., 'insān' for all humans). Mushakkik: an analogical term applying with varying intensity (e.g., 'wujūd' for substances and accidents).
The key differentia is the mode of predication, not the number of meanings.
3
Apply to new examples
Given a term like 'Allah' (a proper name for the Creator), it is ʿalam. 'Kitāb' (book) applies uniformly — mutawāṭiʾ. 'Nūr' (light) is often mushakkik when applied to physical light versus divine light.
Check whether the term names a specific individual, applies equally, or has degrees.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن المصنف لم يستوعب أقسام الاسم، وإليك بيانها : هي أربعة إجمالاً وتسعة تفصيلاً، الأوَّل: ما اتَّحد لفظه ومعناه وتحته ثلاثة؛ العلم والمتواطئ والمشكك . الثَّاني : ما اتَّحد لفظه وتعدّد معناه وتحته أربعة؛ المشترك والمنقول والحقيقة والمجاز . الثَّالث : عكس الثَّاني، أي ما تعدّد لفظه واتَّحد معناه؛ وهو المشترك كغضنفر وهِزَبْر وقشورة للحيوان المفترس. الرَّابع عكس الأوّل ؛ أي: ما تعدّد لفظه ومعناه وهو المتباين كإنسان وفرس.
Know that the author did not exhaust the divisions of the noun. Here is their explanation: They are four in summary and nine in detail. First: what has unified word and meaning, and under it are three: the proper noun, the univocal, and the analogical. Second: what has unified word and multiple meanings, and under it are four: the shared term, the transmitted, the literal, and the metaphorical. Third: the opposite of the second, i.e., what has multiple words and unified meaning, and it is the synonymous, like 'ghaḍanfar', 'hizabr', and 'qashūra' for the predatory animal. Fourth: the opposite of the first, i.e., what has multiple words and multiple meanings, and it is the divergent, like 'insān' and 'faras'.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This footnote provides a comprehensive classification of nouns (asmā') based on the combination of word-form and meaning. It is an enumerative passage intended for memorization. The author (al-Sharnūbī) notes that the main text did not cover all divisions. There are four overarching categories: (1) single word and single meaning: includes proper nouns (ʿalam), univocal terms (mutawāṭi'), and analogical terms (mushakkik). (2) single word and multiple meanings: includes shared terms (mushtarak), transmitted terms (manqūl), literal (ḥaqīqa), and metaphorical (majāz). (3) multiple words and single meaning: this is synonymy (mushtarak – note: here mushtarak is used differently; it means synonymous, not homonymous), e.g., different words for lion. (4) multiple words and multiple meanings: these are divergent terms (mutabāyin), e.g., 'insān' and 'faras'. This classification is foundational for understanding logical and linguistic analysis of terms.

Second Division: Mushtarak, Manqūl, Ḥaqīqa, and Majāz
Footnote enumerates the second division of nouns with four subtypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between homonymous, transmitted, literal, and metaphorical uses of a single word.

Core Idea
When one word has multiple meanings, the source of multiplicity — original sharing, convention shift, or figurative extension — determines the subtype.

The second division covers nouns with one word but multiple meanings. Al-Sharnūbī lists four subtypes: mushtarak (homonym), manqūl (transmitted), ḥaqīqa (literal), and majāz (metaphorical). Each arises from a different cause, and recognizing them is essential for accurate interpretation of Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Recall the second division definition
Second division: one word, multiple meanings (muttaḥid al-lafẓ, mutaʿaddid al-maʿnā). The four subtypes share this property.
2
Learn the four subtypes
Mushtarak: a word with unrelated meanings, e.g., 'ʿayn' (eye, spring, etc.). Manqūl: a word moved from original meaning to a new one by convention, e.g., 'ṣalāt' originally 'supplication' but in Sharīʿa 'specific prayer'. Ḥaqīqa: the original or established literal meaning. Majāz: a meaning derived by relationship, e.g., 'asad' (lion) for a brave man.
The classification depends on the origin of the additional meaning.
3
Apply to Quranic examples
Consider 'mā' in Quran: it can mean 'water' (ḥaqīqa) or 'wealth' (perhaps majāz if extended). The term 'salāt' is manqūl from its root meaning. Words like 'ʿayn' are mushtarak.
Context determines which specific meaning is intended.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن المصنف لم يستوعب أقسام الاسم، وإليك بيانها : هي أربعة إجمالاً وتسعة تفصيلاً، الأوَّل: ما اتَّحد لفظه ومعناه وتحته ثلاثة؛ العلم والمتواطئ والمشكك . الثَّاني : ما اتَّحد لفظه وتعدّد معناه وتحته أربعة؛ المشترك والمنقول والحقيقة والمجاز . الثَّالث : عكس الثَّاني، أي ما تعدّد لفظه واتَّحد معناه؛ وهو المشترك كغضنفر وهِزَبْر وقشورة للحيوان المفترس. الرَّابع عكس الأوّل ؛ أي: ما تعدّد لفظه ومعناه وهو المتباين كإنسان وفرس.
Know that the author did not exhaust the divisions of the noun. Here is their explanation: They are four in summary and nine in detail. First: what has unified word and meaning, and under it are three: the proper noun, the univocal, and the analogical. Second: what has unified word and multiple meanings, and under it are four: the shared term, the transmitted, the literal, and the metaphorical. Third: the opposite of the second, i.e., what has multiple words and unified meaning, and it is the synonymous, like 'ghaḍanfar', 'hizabr', and 'qashūra' for the predatory animal. Fourth: the opposite of the first, i.e., what has multiple words and multiple meanings, and it is the divergent, like 'insān' and 'faras'.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This footnote provides a comprehensive classification of nouns (asmā') based on the combination of word-form and meaning. It is an enumerative passage intended for memorization. The author (al-Sharnūbī) notes that the main text did not cover all divisions. There are four overarching categories: (1) single word and single meaning: includes proper nouns (ʿalam), univocal terms (mutawāṭi'), and analogical terms (mushakkik). (2) single word and multiple meanings: includes shared terms (mushtarak), transmitted terms (manqūl), literal (ḥaqīqa), and metaphorical (majāz). (3) multiple words and single meaning: this is synonymy (mushtarak – note: here mushtarak is used differently; it means synonymous, not homonymous), e.g., different words for lion. (4) multiple words and multiple meanings: these are divergent terms (mutabāyin), e.g., 'insān' and 'faras'. This classification is foundational for understanding logical and linguistic analysis of terms.
