The Condition for Proximate Genus: Complete Common Part
Author defines condition for jins qarib and refutes a counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given genus is proximate or remote by applying the condition of being the complete common part between the essence and all its co-species.

Core Idea
The proximate genus must be the complete common part; any shared attribute outside makes it remote.

In defining an essence, the genus must be the complete common part (tamam al-mushtarak) between the essence and all species under it. A common error is to assume that any correct answer about the essence and its participants is a proximate genus. This lesson examines a counterexample from the tradition and clarifies the condition using authoritative statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposed genus
In the example, 'body' is proposed as the answer to 'What is man, animal, and growing bodies?' Recognize that this is a candidate for the genus of these species.
2
Extract all shared attributes among the species
Man and animal share growth, sensation, and voluntary motion. Growing bodies share growth but not necessarily sensation or motion. So the full set of common attributes includes growth, sensation, and voluntary motion (for some).
3
Check if the proposed genus covers all shared attributes
The genus 'body' does not include growth, sensation, or voluntary motion. These are outside it. Therefore, 'body' is not the complete common part.
4
Conclude whether the genus is proximate or remote
Since 'body' is not the complete common part, it is a remote genus (jins ba'id). The proximate genus would be 'animal' or a higher genus that includes all these attributes.

فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات

وإذا قيلَ ما الإنسان والفرسُ ... إلخ، والحقُّ أنَّ المراد الكُلُّ الإفرادي؛ أي: كُلُّ فردٍ مِنَ المشاركات. قال شيخُ الإسلام حفيد المصنّف في شرحِهِ: "ولقد أحسنَ قُدِّسَ سره حيث ذكر بدل الجميعِ الواقع في عبارتهم لفظ الكُلِّ في حد القريب، فإنَّ الجنس البعيد أيضاً جواب عَنِ المَاهِيَّةِ وَعَنْ جميع المشاركات". حتَّى لو قِيلَ: مَا الإِنسانُ والحيوان والأجسامُ النَّاميةُ؟ فالجواب: الجسمُ، فَيلزم دخول البعيد في تعريف القريب على الوجهِ القريب فيهِ؛ أي: في جميع. فإنَّ الأقرب أنَّ المراد منهُ كَوْنُ السُّؤالِ عَن جَميعِ الأفرادِ دَفعةً؛ لا كونُهَا على سبيل البَدَلِ، والأقربُ في الكُلِّ المراد القريبِ عَن البَعيدِ، بَلْ لا بُدَّ مَعَ ذلكَ من كَوْنِ القريب تمام المشترك بين الماهية، وكُلَّ ما شاركَهَا فِيهِ يدلّ له قولُ السَّيِّدِ: "المعتبر في مطلقِ الجنسِ أنْ يكونَ تمام المشترك بينَ الماهِيَّةِ ونوع آخَرَ؛ سواء كانَ تمام المشترك بالقياس إلى كُلِّ مَا يشارك الماهية في ذلك الجنسِ؛ أَوْ لا". ا.هـ. وقول مير زاهد: "إِنَّ الجنس القريب هو تمامُ الذَّاتي المشترك بين الماهية وجميع المشاركات؛ والجنس البعيد هو تمامُ الذَّاتيّ المشترك بين الماهية وبعض المشاركات؛ لا جميعها". ا.هـ. إذا عَلِمْتَ ذلكَ، تعلمُ أنَّ الصُّورة الموردة وهي: ما الإنسان والحيوانُ والأجسامُ النَّاميةُ المجابةُ بِالجسم؛ ليسَ الجسم هنا باعتبارِ صِدقِهِ على المذكورات جنساً قريباً؛ لكونه ليس تمام المشترك بين الأنواعِ الثَّلاثةِ، فإنَّ المرادَ بِتَمامِ المشترك؛ هو أن لا يكونَ جزءٌ مشترك خارجاً عنه. وهَهُنَا عليه؛ أي: مِنَ الأنواع المندرجةِ تَحتَهُ؛ كَصِدْقِ الحيوان على أنواعه، وهنا ليس كَذَلِكَ. وحينئذ؛ لا داعي لِمَا فَرَّقُوا بِهِ بينَ "كُلِّ" و "جميع"، وأنَّهُما بمعنى واحد، كما قالَ عبد الحكيم: "لم يرد بالجميع بوصف الاجتماع، بلْ أَعمُّ مِنْ أنْ تكون مجموعةٌ أو مُتفرّقةً، فَلَا فرقَ بينَ "كُلِّ" و "جميع". ا.هـ. يعني: أَنَّهُ يصحُ أنْ يقعَ جَواباً عَنِ الأفرادِ دفعةً واحدةً كَمَا مثَّلَ الشَّارحُ بِقولِهِ: وإذا قِيلَ: ما الإنسانُ والفرسُ ... إلخ؟ أو يفرد بأنْ يُقالَ: ما الإنسانُ أو ما الفرسُ؟ فإنَّ هذهِ أمور اعتباريَّةٌ، والحامل للتصويرِ الثَّاني؛ هو الفرارُ مِنَ الصُّورة الموردة، وقد عَلِمْتَ عدم الورود. نعم، إِنَّ لفظ الكُلِّ والجميع في حد ذاتِهِمَا الشَّائع فيهِمَا؛ هو ما ذكره شيخُ الإسلام، لكن في هذا المقام إرادة كُلِّ منهما صحيح. وبهذا تعلم سقوط مَا قالَهُ البعضُ بقولِهِ: "وفيه نَظَر". أَمَّا أَوَّلاً؛ فَلِأَنَّهُ مَبني على أنَّ "جميعَ" يقتضي الاتحاد في الزَّمانِ، والمصنِّفُ لا يراه... إلخ، فإنَّهُ مَبني على تسليم وُرُودِ السُّؤالِ، وأَنَّ مَبنى وُرودِهِ جَمَعَهَا فِي سؤال واحد، وهو مبني على القول باقتضاء لفظ جميع اتحادَ الزَّمانِ، يعني: ولو مُنِعَ اقتضاؤُهَا لَهُ؛ لا يرد؛ إذْ يرجعُ لِلسُّؤال عنها في آنات؛ لا في زمانٍ واحدٍ، فَلَا جَمْعَ في السُّؤالِ فيرجعُ لِمَا أفادَهُ التَّعبيرُ بِالكُلِّ؛ فَلَا أرجحيَّةَ. على أنَّ لكَ أنْ تقول: إِنَّ مَا استشهدَ بِهِ مِنْ قولِهِ: ﴿ فَسَجَدَ الْمَلَيْكَةُ كُلُّهُمْ أَجْمَعُونَ ﴾ [الحجر: ٣٠] غيرُ مَا نَحْنُ فيه؛ لأنَّ ما ذكره في جميع؛ الواقعة في ألفاظ التَّوكيد، أو الواقعة حالاً في نحو: جاؤوا جميعاً، و"جميع" هنا نظيرُ مَا يُقَالُ: أخذتُ مَ الدَّراهم، ونظرتُ في جميع المواد، فَمَفَادُ ذلكَ تَعَلَّقُ الفعل بالمجموع من حيثُ هُوَ، وَأَمَّا كَوْنُهُ في زمانٍ واحدٍ أو لا؛ فَشَيءٌ آخَرُ. على أَنَّهُ قِيلَ باتّحادِ الزَّمانِ فيها، وإنْ حَكَمَ المصنِّفُ بكونه وهماً؛ فإنَّ هذا احتمال يذهب إليهِ الوَهْمُ فيها؛ دونَ كُلِّ من هذه الحيثيَّة كل عليها.
Section: On the Principles of Conceptualizations. And when it is said 'what is man and horse... etc.', the truth is that what is meant is the individual universal, i.e., every individual of the participants. The Shaikh al-Islam, the grandson of the author, said in his commentary: 'He has done well, may his secret be sanctified, where he mentioned instead of 'jami'' (all) occurring in their expression the word 'kull' (every) in the definition of the proximate [genus], for the remote genus also answers about the essence and about all participants.' So that if it were said: 'What is man, animal, and growing bodies?' The answer is: 'body', then the remote would enter into the definition of the proximate in the way of proximate? i.e., in all. For the more likely is that what is meant by it is the asking about all individuals at once, not that they are on the basis of substitution, and the more likely in 'kull' is the individual meaning. So the meaning of the author's statement is not that he asks about the essence and about every participant that combines the question about the essence and about 'kull', but rather in the sense that he asks about the essence and about a participant, then asks about it and about another participant until the question about the essence and about every participant is realized. End quote. The commentator said it, and some transmitted it and objected, and I say: The correctness of the answer about the essence and about the participant is not sufficient to distinguish the proximate from the remote; rather, along with that, it is necessary for the proximate to be the complete common part between the essence and everything that shares with it in it. This is indicated by the statement of al-Sayyid: 'The criterion for the genus in absolute terms is that it be the complete common part between the essence and another species, whether it is the complete common part relative to everything that shares with the essence in that genus or not.' End quote. And the statement of Mir Zahid: 'The proximate genus is the complete essential common part between the essence and all participants; the remote genus is the complete essential common part between the essence and some participants, not all of them.' End quote. When you know that, you know that the presented example, namely: 'What is man, animal, and growing bodies?' answered with 'body' – body here is not, by virtue of being true of the mentioned things, a proximate genus, because it is not the complete common part between the three species. For what is meant by 'complete common part' is that no common part should be outside it. Here, man and animal share in growth, and in sensation and voluntary motion, and these are outside the genus which is body, so it was not the complete common part. And it is considered in the proximate genus that it be the complete common part between everything of which it is true, i.e., of the species under it, like the truth of 'animal' of its species. And here it is not like that. Therefore, there is no need for what they distinguished between 'kull' and 'jami'', and that they have one meaning, as 'Abd al-Hakim said: 'He did not mean by 'jami'' the description of gathering, but rather more general than being collected or dispersed, so there is no difference between 'kull' and 'jami'.' End quote. Meaning, that it is valid for it to be an answer about the individuals at once, as the commentator exemplified by his statement: 'And when it is said: What is man and horse... etc.?' or it is made singular by saying: 'What is man?' or 'What is horse?' For these are matters of consideration, and the incentive for the second picture is to flee from the presented example, and you have known the non-occurrence [of the objection]. Yes, indeed the words 'kull' and 'jami'' in their own common usage is what the Shaikh al-Islam mentioned, but in this context the intention of each of them is correct. And by this you know the fall of what some said: 'There is consideration in it.' As for first, it is built on that 'jami'' necessitates unity in time, and the author does not see it... etc., for it is built on accepting the occurrence of the question, and that the basis of its occurrence is gathering them in one question, and it is built on the view that the word 'jami'' requires unity of time. Meaning, even if its requirement is denied, it does not occur, because it reverts to asking about them in moments, not in one time, so there is no gathering in the question, so it reverts to what the expression with 'kull' provides, so there is no superiority. Moreover, you may say: Indeed, what he cited from His saying: ﴿ فَسَجَدَ الْمَلَيْكَةُ كُلُّهُمْ أَجْمَعُونَ ﴾ [al-Hijr:30] is different from what we are in, because what he mentioned about 'jami'' occurs in expressions of emphasis, or occurring as a circumstantial phrase in like 'they came all' (jami'an), and 'jami'' here is analogous to what is said: 'I took the dirhams' and 'I looked at all the materials.' So the purport of that is the attachment of the action to the aggregate as such; as for whether it is in one time or not, it is another matter. However, it was said that it requires unity of time, even if the author judged it to be a delusion; for this is a probability that delusion inclines to in it, unlike 'kull' from this aspect entirely.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) in which the commentator, likely al-Dasuqi or al-Attar, addresses a specific issue in the definition of the proximate genus (al-jins al-qarib). The passage begins by discussing the interpretation of the phrase 'when it is said: what is man and horse etc.' The author clarifies that what is meant by 'kull' here is the individual universal, i.e., every individual among the participants. He quotes Shaykh al-Islam (the grandson of the original author) praising the substitution of the word 'kull' for 'jami'' in the definition of the proximate genus, because even the remote genus answers about the essence and about all participants. Then an example is given: if one asks 'what is man, animal, and growing bodies?' and answers 'body', the remote genus (body) would enter into the definition of the proximate genus. The commentator argues that the correct interpretation is that the question is about all individuals at once, not one after another. He then states that the correctness of the answer about the essence and about a participant is not enough to distinguish the proximate from the remote genus; rather, the proximate genus must be the 'complete common part' (tamam al-mushtarak) between the essence and everything that shares with it in that genus. This is supported by quotes from al-Sayyid and Mir Zahid. Applying this to the example, 'body' is not a proximate genus for man, animal, and growing bodies because it is not the complete common part; they share additional attributes like growth, sensation, and voluntary motion which are outside the genus 'body'. The proximate genus must be the complete common part among all its species. Therefore, there is no need for the distinction some make between 'kull' and 'jami'; they are synonymous here, as Abd al-Hakim stated: 'jami'' does not imply collection but is more general, so no difference. The commentator then refutes an objection concerning the implication of unity of time by 'jami'', arguing that even if it required unity, it does not affect the point. The example from the Quran (fasaada al-mala'ikatu kulluhum ajma'un) is distinguished from the usage here. The passage concludes that the objection is unfounded.

Key Terms
الجنس القريب
الجنس البعيد
تمام المشترك

Checkpoint
What is the essential condition for a genus to be considered a proximate genus (jins qarib)?
A) It must be the most specific genus in the definition.
B) It must be the complete common part between the essence and all its co-species.
C) It must be the first answer given when asked about the essence.
D) It must be a part of the essence and exclude all differences.

Key Takeaway
A genus is proximate only if it is the complete common part shared by the essence and all its co-species; any missing shared attribute reduces it to a remote genus.

The Distinction Between 'Kull' and 'Jami'' in Logical Definitions
Author discusses the synonymy of kull and jami' and refutes a counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why 'kull' and 'jami'' are considered synonymous in the context of defining the proximate genus, and refute the objection based on unity of time.

Core Idea
'Kull' and 'jami'' are synonyms in this logical context; the temporal unity objection is a misunderstanding.

Some logicians distinguish between 'kull' (every) and 'jami'' (all), claiming that 'jami'' implies unity of time. This lesson examines the commentator's refutation, supported by 'Abd al-Hakim and the actual usage in definitions. We will see that the distinction is irrelevant and that both terms serve the same function in expressing the condition for the proximate genus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
Some argue that 'jami'' requires unity of time, meaning all individuals must be considered together, whereas 'kull' can be considered individually. This leads to a distinction that affects the definition of proximate genus.
2
Examine the commentator's response
The commentator, citing 'Abd al-Hakim, states that 'jami'' was not meant to imply gathering; rather it is more general, covering both collected and dispersed situations. Therefore, 'kull' and 'jami'' are synonymous.
3
Analyze the Quranic example (al-Hijr:30)
The objection uses the verse 'fasaada al-mala'ikatu kulluhum ajma'un'. The commentator explains that this usage is different: here 'jami'' is an emphatic particle, not a logical quantifier. In logical definitions, 'jami'' functions like 'kull' without temporal connotation.
4
Conclude the synonymy in this context
Thus, there is no need to distinguish them in the definition of proximate genus. The objection based on unity of time does not hold because 'jami'' can apply to multiple individual questions as well as a single collective question.

فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات

وإذا قيلَ ما الإنسان والفرسُ ... إلخ، والحقُّ أنَّ المراد الكُلُّ الإفرادي؛ أي: كُلُّ فردٍ مِنَ المشاركات. قال شيخُ الإسلام حفيد المصنّف في شرحِهِ: "ولقد أحسنَ قُدِّسَ سره حيث ذكر بدل الجميعِ الواقع في عبارتهم لفظ الكُلِّ في حد القريب، فإنَّ الجنس البعيد أيضاً جواب عَنِ المَاهِيَّةِ وَعَنْ جميع المشاركات". حتَّى لو قِيلَ: مَا الإِنسانُ والحيوان والأجسامُ النَّاميةُ؟ فالجواب: الجسمُ، فَيلزم دخول البعيد في تعريف القريب على الوجهِ القريب فيهِ؛ أي: في جميع. فإنَّ الأقرب أنَّ المراد منهُ كَوْنُ السُّؤالِ عَن جَميعِ الأفرادِ دَفعةً؛ لا كونُهَا على سبيل البَدَلِ، والأقربُ في الكُلِّ المراد الإفرادي. فليس معنى كلام المصنّفِ أَنَّهُ يسألُ عَن المَاهِيَّةِ وَعَن كُلِّ مُشارك؛ يجمع السُّؤال عن الماهية والكُلِّ، بَلْ بمعنى أنَّه يسألُ عَن المَاهِيَّةِ وَعَنْ مُشارك، ثمَّ يسألُ عنها وعَنْ مُشارك آخَرَ حَتَّى يتحقَّقَ السُّؤالُ عَن المَاهِيَّةِ وَعَنْ كُلِّ مُشارك.ا.هـ. قالَه المحشّي، ونقله البعض واعترض، وأنا أقولُ: ليسَ صِحَّةُ الجَوابِ عَنِ الماهِيَّةِ وَعَن المشاركِ كافيةً في تمييز القريبِ عَن البَعيدِ، بَلْ لا بُدَّ مَعَ ذلكَ من كَوْنِ القريب تمام المشترك بين الماهية، وكُلَّ ما شاركَهَا فِيهِ يدلّ له قولُ السَّيِّدِ: "المعتبر في مطلقِ الجنسِ أنْ يكونَ تمام المشترك بينَ الماهِيَّةِ ونوع آخَرَ؛ سواء كانَ تمام المشترك بالقياس إلى كُلِّ مَا يشارك الماهية في ذلك الجنسِ؛ أَوْ لا". ا.هـ. وقول مير زاهد: "إِنَّ الجنس القريب هو تمامُ الذَّاتي المشترك بين الماهية وجميع المشاركات؛ والجنس البعيد هو تمامُ الذَّاتيّ المشترك بين الماهية وبعض المشاركات؛ لا جميعها". ا.هـ. إذا عَلِمْتَ ذلكَ، تعلمُ أنَّ الصُّورة الموردة وهي: ما الإنسان والحيوانُ والأجسامُ النَّاميةُ المجابةُ بِالجسم؛ ليسَ الجسم هنا باعتبارِ صِدقِهِ على المذكورات جنساً قريباً؛ لكونه ليس تمام المشترك بين الأنواعِ الثَّلاثةِ، فإنَّ المرادَ بِتَمامِ المشترك؛ هو أن لا يكونَ جزءٌ مشترك خارجاً عنه. وهَهُنَا الإنسانُ والحيوانُ اشتركا في النمو، وفي الإحساس والحركة الإرادية، وهذهِ خارجة عن الجنس الذي هو الجسمُ، فَلَمْ يكن تمام المشترك. وقد اعتبر في الجنسِ القريبِ أنْ يكونَ تمام المشترك بين جميع ما يصدق عليه؛ أي: مِنَ الأنواع المندرجةِ تَحتَهُ؛ كَصِدْقِ الحيوان على أنواعه، وهنا ليس كَذَلِكَ. وحينئذ؛ لا داعي لِمَا فَرَّقُوا بِهِ بينَ "كُلِّ" و "جميع"، وأنَّهُما بمعنى واحد، كما قالَ عبد الحكيم: "لم يرد بالجميع بوصف الاجتماع، بلْ أَعمُّ مِنْ أنْ تكون مجموعةٌ أو مُتفرّقةً، فَلَا فرقَ بينَ "كُلِّ" و "جميع". ا.هـ. يعني: أَنَّهُ يصحُ أنْ يقعَ جَواباً عَنِ الأفرادِ دفعةً واحدةً كَمَا مثَّلَ الشَّارحُ بِقولِهِ: وإذا قِيلَ: ما الإنسانُ والفرسُ ... إلخ؟ أو يفرد بأنْ يُقالَ: ما الإنسانُ أو ما الفرسُ؟ فإنَّ هذهِ أمور اعتباريَّةٌ، والحامل للتصويرِ الثَّاني؛ هو الفرارُ مِنَ الصُّورة الموردة، وقد عَلِمْتَ عدم الورود. نعم، إِنَّ لفظ الكُلِّ والجميع في حد ذاتِهِمَا الشَّائع فيهِمَا؛ هو ما ذكره شيخُ الإسلام، لكن في هذا المقام إرادة كُلِّ منهما صحيح. وبهذا تعلم سقوط مَا قالَهُ البعضُ بقولِهِ: "وفيه نَظَر". أَمَّا أَوَّلاً؛ فَلِأَنَّهُ مَبني على أنَّ "جميعَ" يقتضي الاتحاد في الزَّمانِ، والمصنِّفُ لا يراه... إلخ، فإنَّهُ مَبني على تسليم وُرُودِ السُّؤالِ، وأَنَّ مَبنى وُرودِهِ جَمَعَهَا فِي سؤال واحد، وهو مبني على القول باقتضاء لفظ جميع اتحادَ الزَّمانِ، يعني: ولو مُنِعَ اقتضاؤُهَا لَهُ؛ لا يرد؛ إذْ يرجعُ لِلسُّؤال عنها في آنات؛ لا في زمانٍ واحدٍ، فَلَا جَمْعَ في السُّؤالِ فيرجعُ لِمَا أفادَهُ التَّعبيرُ بِالكُلِّ؛ فَلَا أرجحيَّةَ. على أنَّ لكَ أنْ تقول: إِنَّ مَا استشهدَ بِهِ مِنْ قولِهِ: ﴿ فَسَجَدَ الْمَلَيْكَةُ كُلُّهُمْ أَجْمَعُونَ ﴾ [الحجر: ٣٠] غيرُ مَا نَحْنُ فيه؛ لأنَّ ما ذكره في جميع؛ الواقعة في ألفاظ التَّوكيد، أو الواقعة حالاً في نحو: جاؤوا جميعاً، و"جميع" هنا نظيرُ مَا يُقَالُ: أخذتُ مَ الدَّراهم، ونظرتُ في جميع المواد، فَمَفَادُ ذلكَ تَعَلَّقُ الفعل بالمجموع من حيثُ هُوَ، وَأَمَّا كَوْنُهُ في زمانٍ واحدٍ أو لا؛ فَشَيءٌ آخَرُ. على أَنَّهُ قِيلَ باتّحادِ الزَّمانِ فيها، وإنْ حَكَمَ المصنِّفُ بكونه وهماً؛ فإنَّ هذا احتمال يذهب إليهِ الوَهْمُ فيها؛ دونَ كُلِّ من هذه الحيثيَّة كل عليها.
Section: On the Principles of Conceptualizations. And when it is said 'what is man and horse... etc.', the truth is that what is meant is the individual universal, i.e., every individual of the participants. The Shaikh al-Islam, the grandson of the author, said in his commentary: 'He has done well, may his secret be sanctified, where he mentioned instead of 'jami'' (all) occurring in their expression the word 'kull' (every) in the definition of the proximate [genus], for the remote genus also answers about the essence and about all participants.' So that if it were said: 'What is man, animal, and growing bodies?' The answer is: 'body', then the remote would enter into the definition of the proximate in the way of proximate? i.e., in all. For the more likely is that what is meant by it is the asking about all individuals at once, not that they are on the basis of substitution, and the more likely in 'kull' is the individual meaning. So the meaning of the author's statement is not that he asks about the essence and about every participant that combines the question about the essence and about 'kull', but rather in the sense that he asks about the essence and about a participant, then asks about it and about another participant until the question about the essence and about every participant is realized. End quote. The commentator said it, and some transmitted it and objected, and I say: The correctness of the answer about the essence and about the participant is not sufficient to distinguish the proximate from the remote; rather, along with that, it is necessary for the proximate to be the complete common part between the essence and everything that shares with it in it. This is indicated by the statement of al-Sayyid: 'The criterion for the genus in absolute terms is that it be the complete common part between the essence and another species, whether it is the complete common part relative to everything that shares with the essence in that genus or not.' End quote. And the statement of Mir Zahid: 'The proximate genus is the complete essential common part between the essence and all participants; the remote genus is the complete essential common part between the essence and some participants, not all of them.' End quote. When you know that, you know that the presented example, namely: 'What is man, animal, and growing bodies?' answered with 'body' – body here is not, by virtue of being true of the mentioned things, a proximate genus, because it is not the complete common part between the three species. For what is meant by 'complete common part' is that no common part should be outside it. Here, man and animal share in growth, and in sensation and voluntary motion, and these are outside the genus which is body, so it was not the complete common part. And it is considered in the proximate genus that it be the complete common part between everything of which it is true, i.e., of the species under it, like the truth of 'animal' of its species. And here it is not like that. Therefore, there is no need for what they distinguished between 'kull' and 'jami'', and that they have one meaning, as 'Abd al-Hakim said: 'He did not mean by 'jami'' the description of gathering, but rather more general than being collected or dispersed, so there is no difference between 'kull' and 'jami'.' End quote. Meaning, that it is valid for it to be an answer about the individuals at once, as the commentator exemplified by his statement: 'And when it is said: What is man and horse... etc.?' or it is made singular by saying: 'What is man?' or 'What is horse?' For these are matters of consideration, and the incentive for the second picture is to flee from the presented example, and you have known the non-occurrence [of the objection]. Yes, indeed the words 'kull' and 'jami'' in their own common usage is what the Shaikh al-Islam mentioned, but in this context the intention of each of them is correct. And by this you know the fall of what some said: 'There is consideration in it.' As for first, it is built on that 'jami'' necessitates unity in time, and the author does not see it... etc., for it is built on accepting the occurrence of the question, and that the basis of its occurrence is gathering them in one question, and it is built on the view that the word 'jami'' requires unity of time. Meaning, even if its requirement is denied, it does not occur, because it reverts to asking about them in moments, not in one time, so there is no gathering in the question, so it reverts to what the expression with 'kull' provides, so there is no superiority. Moreover, you may say: Indeed, what he cited from His saying: ﴿ فَسَجَدَ الْمَلَيْكَةُ كُلُّهُمْ أَجْمَعُونَ ﴾ [al-Hijr:30] is different from what we are in, because what he mentioned about 'jami'' occurs in expressions of emphasis, or occurring as a circumstantial phrase in like 'they came all' (jami'an), and 'jami'' here is analogous to what is said: 'I took the dirhams' and 'I looked at all the materials.' So the purport of that is the attachment of the action to the aggregate as such; as for whether it is in one time or not, it is another matter. However, it was said that it requires unity of time, even if the author judged it to be a delusion; for this is a probability that delusion inclines to in it, unlike 'kull' from this aspect entirely.

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) in which the commentator, likely al-Dasuqi or al-Attar, addresses a specific issue in the definition of the proximate genus (al-jins al-qarib). The passage begins by discussing the interpretation of the phrase 'when it is said: what is man and horse etc.' The author clarifies that what is meant by 'kull' here is the individual universal, i.e., every individual among the participants. He quotes Shaykh al-Islam (the grandson of the original author) praising the substitution of the word 'kull' for 'jami'' in the definition of the proximate genus, because even the remote genus answers about the essence and about all participants. Then an example is given: if one asks 'what is man, animal, and growing bodies?' and answers 'body', the remote genus (body) would enter into the definition of the proximate genus. The commentator argues that the correct interpretation is that the question is about all individuals at once, not one after another. He then states that the correctness of the answer about the essence and about a participant is not enough to distinguish the proximate from the remote genus; rather, the proximate genus must be the 'complete common part' (tamam al-mushtarak) between the essence and everything that shares with it in that genus. This is supported by quotes from al-Sayyid and Mir Zahid. Applying this to the example, 'body' is not a proximate genus for man, animal, and growing bodies because it is not the complete common part; they share additional attributes like growth, sensation, and voluntary motion which are outside the genus 'body'. The proximate genus must be the complete common part among all its species. Therefore, there is no need for the distinction some make between 'kull' and 'jami'; they are synonymous here, as Abd al-Hakim stated: 'jami'' does not imply collection but is more general, so no difference. The commentator then refutes an objection concerning the implication of unity of time by 'jami'', arguing that even if it required unity, it does not affect the point. The example from the Quran (fasaada al-mala'ikatu kulluhum ajma'un) is distinguished from the usage here. The passage concludes that the objection is unfounded.

What does the commentator (and 'Abd al-Hakim) claim about the relationship between 'kull' and 'jami' in the context of defining the proximate genus?
A) 'Jami' requires unity of time, while 'kull' does not.

Identifying the Proximate Genus
Definition of proximate genus with example and commentary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the proximate genus for any given species by applying the condition that it answers 'What is it?' for the species and all its co-species.

Core Idea
The proximate genus is the immediate predicate shared by a species and all its co-species.

This lesson unpacks the condition for a genus to be proximate (qarīb) rather than remote (baʿīd). The author provides a clear criterion: a genus is proximate if it serves as the answer to the 'What is it?' question for the species and for every other species that shares that genus. Understanding this condition is essential for constructing correct definitions (ḥudūd) in any Islamic science.

Guided Path
1
Identify the species and the question
Start with the species you are analyzing, e.g., 'human' (insān), and ask 'What is it?' (mā huwa). The expected answer is the genus.
2
List the co-species of that genus
Determine which other species share the same immediate genus with the species in question. For human, co-species include horse (faras), dog, etc., all sharing the genus 'animal'.
These are the mushārakāt (partners) mentioned in the text.
3
Find a term that answers for both
Look for a term that answers 'What is it?' for the species and for each of its co-species. 'Animal' answers for human, horse, and others, so it qualifies as a proximate genus candidate.
If the same term works for all, it satisfies the condition.
4
Check if it answers for all co-species
Verify that the term answers not just for some co-species but for every individual co-species on an alternative basis. This ensures the genus is truly common to all.
The text emphasizes tamām al-mushtarak (complete commonality).
5
Distinguish from remote genus
If the term fails for some co-species (e.g., 'body' answers for human but not for horse in the same way), it is a remote genus. Only the immediate genus passing the condition is proximate.
Example: 'body' is remote; 'animal' is proximate for human.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي مَبَادِئِ التَّصَوُّرَاتِ. فَإِنْ كَانَ الْجَوَابُ عَنِ الْمَاهِيَّةِ وَعَنْ بَعْضِ الْمُشَارَكَاتِ) أَيْ: مُشَارَكَاتِ الْمَاهِيَّةِ (هُوَ الْجَوَابُ عَنْهَا) أَيْ: عَنِ الْمَاهِيَّةِ، (وَعَنِ الْكُلِّ) أَيْ: كُلِّ الْمُشَارَكَاتِ؛ فَقَرِيبٌ كَالْحَيَوَانِ، فَإِنَّهُ جَوَابٌ عَنِ الْإِنْسَانِ وَعَنِ الْفَرَسِ. شَرْحُ الدَّسُوقِي: (قَوْلُهُ: فَإِنْ كَانَ الْجَوَابُ) أَيْ: عَنِ السُّؤَالِ بِـ "مَا هُوَ" جَوَابًا عَنِ الْمَاهِيَّةِ، أَيْ: جَوَابًا عَنِ السُّؤَالِ عَنِ الْمَاهِيَّةِ النَّوْعِيَّةِ الَّتِي الْجِنْسُ جِنْسٌ بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَيْهَا، وَعَنْ بَعْضِ مُشَارَكَاتِهَا فِي ذَلِكَ الْجِنْسِ. وَقَوْلُهُ: (هُوَ الْجَوَابُ عَنْهَا) أَيْ: عَنِ السُّؤَالِ عَنْهَا، وَعَنِ الْكُلِّ. قَالَ بَعْضٌ: لَوْ قَالَ الْمُصَنِّفُ: فَإِنْ كَانَ جَوَابًا عَنِ الْمَاهِيَّةِ وَعَنِ الْكُلِّ، أَوْ قَالَ: فَإِنْ كَانَ الْجَوَابُ عَنْ كُلِّ مُشَارَكٍ وَاحِدٍ، فَقَرِيبٌ كَالْحَيَوَانِ، وَإِلَّا فَبَعِيدٌ كَالْجِسْمِ، لَكَانَ أَخْصَرَ وَأَظْهَرَ. (قَوْلُهُ: عَنِ الْمَاهِيَّةِ) أَيْ: كَالْإِنْسَانِ. (قَوْلُهُ: وَعَنْ بَعْضِ الْمُشَارَكَاتِ) أَيْ: كَالْفَرَسِ. (قَوْلُهُ: عَنِ الْمَاهِيَّةِ) أَيْ: عَنِ السُّؤَالِ عَنِ الْمَاهِيَّةِ الَّتِي الْجِنْسُ جِنْسٌ بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَيْهَا. (قَوْلُهُ: وَعَنِ الْكُلِّ) أَيْ: كُلِّ الْمُشَارَكَاتِ فِي الْجِسْمِ النَّامِي، وَقَوْلُهُ: (وَعَنِ الْكُلِّ) أَيِ: الْجَمِيعِي، بِحَيْثُ يُجَابُ عَنْهَا وَعَنْ كُلِّ فَرْدٍ عَلَى الْبَدَلِيَّةِ. وَقَالَ الْعَطَّارُ: مُسْتَقِيمٌ، كَيْفَ وَقَدِ اتَّفَقُوا عَلَى أَنَّ الْمُرَادَ بِهِ تَمَامُ الْمُشْتَرَكِ؟ فَبَعْدَ هَذَا الِاتِّفَاقِ وَالتَّصْرِيحِ بِهِ مِنْهُمْ تُرْتَكَبُ هَذِهِ التَّرْدِيدَاتُ، وَأَشْنَعُ مِنْ ذَلِكَ قَوْلُهُ: وَالَّذِي عِنْدِي، وَيَأْتِي بِنَحْوِ مَا نَقَلْنَاهُ سَابِقًا، فَإِنَّ هَذَا لَيْسَ مِنْ عِنْدِهِ؛ بَلْ مِنْ عِنْدِ غَيْرِهِ، فَهَذَا كَافْتِخَارِ الْعَقِيمِ بِوَلَدِ غَيْرِهِ، وَلَوْ لَمْ يَقُلْ هَذَا غَيْرُهُ وَانْفَرَدَ بِهِ هُوَ، لَا يُقْبَلُ مِنْهُ؛ لِأَنَّهُ لَيْسَ مِنَ الْمُدَوِّنِينَ لِلْفَنِّ، وَلَا مِمَّنْ يَضَعُ الِاصْطِلَاحَاتِ، بَلْ هُوَ مِنْ أَحَادِ النَّقَلَةِ لِكَلَامِ الْغَيْرِ، فَوُقُوفُ أَمْثَالِنَا عَلَى حَدِّهِ أَوْفَقُ لَهُ وَأَمْثَلُ. وَلِلَّهِ دَرُّ الْقَائِلِ: إِذَا الْتَقَى الْخَيْلُ فِي مَعْسَكَرِهَا فَكَيْفَ حَالُ الْبَعُوضِ فِي الْوَسَطِ. (قَوْلُهُ: فَقَرِيبٌ) أَيْ: فَهُوَ جِنْسٌ قَرِيبٌ؛ لِأَنَّهُ الِاسْمُ، وَكَذَا يُقَالُ فِي بَعِيدٍ.
Chapter: On the principles of concepts. If the answer concerning the essence and concerning some of the shared attributes (i.e., the shared attributes of the essence) is the answer concerning it (i.e., concerning the essence) and concerning all (i.e., all the shared attributes), then it is proximate, like 'animal', for it is an answer for 'human' and for 'horse'. Commentary of al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'If the answer') meaning: concerning the question 'What is it?', an answer concerning the essence, i.e., an answer concerning the question about the specific essence to which the genus is a genus relative to it, and concerning some of its partners in that genus. And his statement: 'it is the answer concerning it' meaning: concerning the question about it, and concerning all. Some said: if the author had said: 'If it is an answer concerning the essence and concerning all', or said: 'If the answer concerning every individual partner, then it is proximate like animal, otherwise it is remote like body', it would be more concise and clearer. (His statement: 'concerning the essence') i.e., like 'human'. (His statement: 'and concerning some of the shared attributes') i.e., like 'horse'. (His statement: 'concerning the essence') i.e., concerning the question about the essence to which the genus is a genus relative to it. (His statement: 'and concerning all') i.e., all the shared attributes in the growing body, and his statement: ('and concerning all') meaning: the comprehensive, such that it is answered concerning it and concerning every individual on an alternative basis. And al-Attar said: It is correct, how have they agreed that what is meant by it is the entirety of the shared attribute? After this agreement and their explicit statement of it, these vacillations are committed. And more shameful than that is his statement: 'what is with me', and he comes with something like what we transmitted before, for this is not from him; rather it is from someone else. So this is like the boasting of the barren with the child of another. And if no one else had said this and he was unique in it, it would not be accepted from him, because he is not among the codifiers of the discipline, nor among those who place the technical terms; rather he is among the mere transmitters of the speech of others. So it is more fitting and more appropriate for the likes of us to stop at his limit. How excellent is the saying of the poet: 'When the horses meet in their camp, what is the state of the mosquito in the middle?' (His statement: 'so it is proximate') i.e., it is a proximate genus, because it is the name, and likewise it is said concerning 'remote'.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage defines the condition for a genus to be proximate (qarib) and distinguishes it from remote (ba'id). The author states that if the answer to the question 'What is it?' (ma huwa) for a specific essence (e.g., human) and for some of its co-species (e.g., horse) is the same answer for the essence and for all co-species, then that genus is proximate. The example given is 'animal', which answers for both human and horse. If not, the genus is remote, like 'body'. The commentary by al-Dasuqi expands this by clarifying that the question is about the specific essence to which the genus relates. He notes a suggested alternative wording that would be more concise. The commentary then specifies examples: the essence is like human, the co-species is like horse. It also explains that 'all shared attributes' refers to all partners in the growing body (al-jism al-nami), and that the answer must cover each individual on an alternative basis. Finally, al-Attar criticizes unnecessary disputes and the false attribution of original ideas, emphasizing the importance of relying on established authorities.

جنس قريب
مشاركات
تمام المشترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Animal (hayawān)
C) Substance (jawhar)
D) Growing body (jism nāmi)

Key Takeaway
A genus is proximate if and only if it answers the 'What is it?' question identically for the species and all its co-species.

Two Meanings of Mahiyya: Essence vs. Definitional Answer
Author distinguishes two meanings of mahiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two meanings of mahiyya and identify which one is used in definitions.

Core Idea
Mahiyya has two senses: the thing itself and the answer to 'what is it?' – only the second excludes individuals and species.

In Islamic logic, the term 'mahiyya' (quiddity) is foundational for definitions. However, it is ambiguous. The commentator Abu al-Fath clarifies that mahiyya has two well-known meanings, and only the second is suitable for definitions. This distinction is crucial for precision in defining terms across the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Find the two definitions of mahiyya in the text: 'what the thing is itself' and 'what is answered to the question what is it?'. Notice that the first is about intrinsic essence, the second about a definitional response.
2
Compare implications
Check what each meaning excludes: the first does not exclude individuals, the second excludes both individuals and species. Understand why: because the answer to 'what is it?' must be a universal genus.
3
Determine the definitional meaning
The author states that mahiyya in definitions is the second meaning. Notice that he removed the restrictor 'awwaliyya' (primitive) to indicate this.
4
Apply to an example
Take a term like 'salat' (prayer). If we define it as 'actions that begin with takbir and end with taslim', which meaning of mahiyya does this correspond to? Is it the essence or the answer to 'what is it'?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات
هذا وفي حاشية أبي الفَتْحِ؛ أنَّ لِلماهِيَّةِ مَعنيَيْنِ مَشْهُورَيْنِ؛ أحدهما : مَا الشَّيءُ هوَ هوَ، والآخَرُ : مَا يُجابُ بِهِ عَنِ السُّؤالِ بِمَا هوَ، وهو بالمعنى الأول: لا يستلزمُ الكُلِّيَّةَ أَصْلاً؛ فضلاً عن دلالتها عليها؛ التزاماً لصدقها على الجزئيات الحقيقيَّةِ، فَهِيَ لا تُخرِجُ الشَّخصَ، وبالمعنى الثَّاني تُخرِج الشَّخصَ والصِّنْفَ أيضاً؛ إذْ لا يصحُ أنْ يُجابَ بِشيءٍ منهما عن السُّؤال بما هو، والحقُّ أَنَّ الماهيَّةَ هُنَا بالمعنى الثَّاني: ولا حاجة إلى قيدٍ آخَرَ لإخراج الصنفِ، وللتنبيه على هذا؛ حذف المصنِّفُ مِنَ التَّعريفِ قيد الأوَّليَّةِ، ولم يذكر قيداً آخَرَ ا.هـ. وهو كلام حسن، تندفع به التَّكلُّفات السابقة، غير أنَّهُ نقضه مير زاهد بأنَّ الحقِّ أنَّ لفظ الماهيةِ مُشتق مِنَ هَاتَيْنِ العبارتين، ومعناها الحقيقي هو الأمر المعقولُ؛ أي: الحاصل في العقل من غير اعتبار الوجودِ الخارجي؛ كَمَا أشار إليه المحقِّقُ الطُّوسِيُّ في التَّجريد، وهذا المعنى يشملُ الصِّنْفَ، فَلَا بُدَّ هَهُنا لإخراجهِ مِن قَيْد.
Section: On the principles of concepts. This is in the gloss of Abi al-Fath: that mahiyya has two well-known meanings: one is 'what the thing is itself,' and the other is 'what is answered with the question 'what is it?''. By the first meaning, it does not imply universality at all, let alone indicate it, because it is true of real particulars, so it does not exclude the individual. By the second meaning, it excludes the individual and also the species, because it is not valid to answer with either of them to the question 'what is it?'. The truth is that mahiyya here is by the second meaning, and there is no need for another restriction to exclude the species. To indicate this, the author removed the restriction of 'primitive' ('awwaliyya) from the definition and did not mention another restriction. This is good speech that repels the previous complications, except that Mir Zahid refuted it by saying that the truth is that the word mahiyya is derived from these two expressions, and its real meaning is the intelligible thing, i.e., what is obtained in the mind without consideration of external existence, as indicated by al-Muhaqqiq al-Tusi in al-Tajrid. This meaning includes the species, so here a restriction is necessary to exclude it.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and critical commentary on the concept of mahiyya (quiddity). It opens by establishing that mahiyya has two well-known meanings: first, 'what the thing is itself' (ma al-shay' huwa huwa), which refers to the intrinsic essence without consideration of universality; second, 'what is answered to the question 'what is it?'' (ma yujab bihi 'an al-su'al bi ma huwa), which is the definitional response that applies to a kind. The author notes that the first meaning does not imply universality at all, so it does not exclude individual particulars. The second meaning, however, excludes both the individual and the species (al-sinf) because neither can be a valid answer to 'what is it?' for a higher kind. The truth, according to this gloss, is that mahiyya in the context of definitions is taken in the second meaning, and thus no additional restriction is needed to exclude the species. The original author's removal of the term 'awwaliyya (primitive) signals this. The passage then records a critique by Mir Zahid: he argues that the literal meaning of mahiyya is 'the intelligible thing' (al-amr al-ma'qul), i.e., what exists in the mind irrespective of external existence, as al-Tusi stated in al-Tajrid. This meaning includes the species, so a restriction is still needed to exclude it. The commentary thus presents an internal debate on whether mahiyya inherently excludes subspecies or requires an explicit restrictor, reflecting the precision required in defining universal terms.

Key Terms
الماهية
الصنف
الكلي

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which meaning of mahiyya excludes both the individual and the species (sinf)?
A) The first meaning: 'what the thing is itself'
B) The second meaning: 'what is answered to the question what is it?'
C) Both meanings equally
D) Neither meaning

Key Takeaway
Mahiyya in definitions is the second meaning: the answer to 'what is it?', which excludes individuals and species.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the definition of 'sawm' (fasting) as 'abstaining from eating, drinking, and sexual activity from dawn to sunset'. Determine which meaning of mahiyya is being used and explain why.

The Debate on the Need for an Additional Restrictor for Mahiyya
Mir Zahid critiques the sufficiency of the second meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze Mir Zahid's objection to the definition of mahiyya and evaluate whether an additional restrictor is needed.

Core Idea
Mir Zahid argued that mahiyya, as the intelligible concept, includes species, so a restrictor is necessary.

The previous lesson established that mahiyya in definitions is taken in the second meaning. However, Mir Zahid raises a powerful objection: the literal meaning of mahiyya ('the intelligible thing') still encompasses the species. This forces a re-examination of whether the definition requires an explicit restrictor.

Guided Path
1
Understand Mir Zahid's argument
Mir Zahid asserts that mahiyya is derived from 'what is it?' and means the intelligible concept in the mind, which includes the species. Find the basis for this claim (al-Tusi's Tajrid).
2
Identify the disagreement
The original gloss claimed no additional restrictor is needed because the second meaning already excludes species. Mir Zahid says that since the term mahiyya itself includes species, a restrictor is needed.
3
Evaluate both positions
Consider which argument is stronger. Does the second meaning inherently exclude species, or does it require external restriction? Use examples from fiqh to test.
4
Synthesize
Reflect on how this debate illustrates the precision required in logical definitions. The passage does not resolve the debate but highlights the complexity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

In a debate about the definition of 'ilm' (knowledge), one party argues that the term itself includes both necessary and acquired knowledge. Construct an objection similar to Mir Zahid's, arguing that an additional restrictor is needed to exclude one type.
?
Examine a definition from al-Jurjani's Definitions (e.g., 'al-zawja' as the female spouse) and decide if it requires an additional restrictor to exclude certain categories.
